(ملبوسين على طهر تام وقت الحدث (٣) ، فلو توضأ وضوءاً غير مرتب فغسلَ الرجلين ، ثم لبس الخفين ، ثُمَّ غسل باقي الأعضاء، ثم أحدث. أو توضأ وضوءاً مُرتباً ، فغسل رجلَهُ اليُمْنى وأدخلَها في الحُفٌ ، ثُمَّ غسل رجلَهُ الْيُسْرى وأدخلها في الحف ليس له طهارة تامة في الصورة الأولى إذا لَبِسَ الخُفِّيْن، وفي الصورة الثانية" إِذا لَبِسَ اليُمْنى
[1]قوله : ملبوسين ؛ صفةٌ لكلِّ من الخفين والجرموقين والجوربين.
(۲) قوله : طهر نام ؛ احترز به عمّا إذا لبسهما المتيمم أو المتوضئ بنبيذ التمر، فإنّه لا يمسح، وصاحب العذر مع العذر، فإنّه لا يمسح خارج الوقت. كذا في «جامع الرموز ) ، وغيره.
[٣] قوله : وقت الحدث (۲) ؛ ظرف لتام ؛ أي يشترط أن يكون الطهر التام عند الحدث اللاحق بعد لبس الخفين، ولا يشترط أن يكون تاماً . عند اللبس وقبل الحدث أيضاً.
[٤]قوله : غسل باقي الأعضاء ؛ المراد بالغسل أعم من الحقيقي والحكمي، فيشمل مسح الرأس؛ لكونه غسلاً حكميَّاً ، فلا يرد أن باقي الأعضاء إن أريد به ما يشمل الرأس أيضاً فهو غير صحيح؛ لأنه لا يغسلُ في الوضوء، وإن أريد به ما عداه يكون الكلام خالياً عن ذكر المسح. [0]قوله : في الصورة الأولى ؛ وهي ما إذا غسل الرجلين أولاً ، ولبس الخفين ثمَّ أتم وضوءه، ففي هذه الصورة الموجودة عند حدوث اللبس ليس إلا غسلُ الرجلين لا الطهارة التامة لإتمامه الوضوء بعده ، نعم يصدق عليه أنه ذو طهارة كاملة عند الحدث اللاحق، وهو زمان بقاء اللبس، لا زمان حدوثه. [٦]قوله : وفي الصورة الثانية ؛ وهي ما إذا توضأ وضوءاً مُرتَّباً ولم يتمه ، بل لَبسَ
Book of Purification / Wiping over Khuffs
(That are soled, or covered with leather) such that if they are thick but not soled or covered with leather, it is not permissible according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to his two companions. And it is narrated from him that he retracted to their opinion, and according to this fatwa is given.
[Comment: 'soled' (mun'al) means having leather attached to the bottom like a sandal. 'Covered with leather' (mujallad) means having leather attached to both top and bottom. Thus in al-Kifayah.
[2] His saying: 'such that if they are' is a sub-branch of the author's restriction of wiping over socks to those that are soled or leather-covered, as a prevention of the absolute. The summary: If the socks are soled or leather-covered, wiping over them is permissible by agreement of our companions. If they are neither soled nor leather-covered, they differed: Abu Hanifa prevented it in his old opinion, arguing that it is not possible to maintain walking in them unless they are soled or leather-covered, so they are not analogous to the khuff. His two companions permitted it based on the fact that if they are thick, continuous walking is possible, so they resemble the khuff. If they are not thick as well, there is agreement that wiping over them is not permissible.
[3] His saying: 'and it is narrated from him that he retracted' means at the end of his life, nine days before his death, or it is said three days. Thus in Majma' al-Anhur.
(Worn upon a complete purification at the time of the event) If one performed an unsequential ablution and washed the feet, then put on khuffs, then washed the remaining limbs, then had an event; or if one performed a sequential ablution, washing the right foot and inserting it into the khuff, then washing the left foot and inserting it into the khuff, he does not have a complete purification in the first scenario when he put on both khuffs, and in the second scenario when he put on the right one.
[1] His saying: 'worn' is an attribute for each of the khuff, sock, and boot.
[2] His saying: 'complete purification' is a precaution against the case of one who performs dry ablution (tayammum) or ablution with date-wine, for he does not wipe; and the excused person with his excuse does not wipe outside the time. Thus in Jami' al-Rumuz and others.
[3] His saying: 'at the time of the event' is a circumstance for 'complete'; i.e., it is a condition that the complete purification be at the time of the later event after putting on the khuffs, and it is not required that it be complete at the time of putting on before the event as well.
[4] His saying: 'washed the remaining limbs' means washing in both the literal and figurative sense, so it includes wiping the head because it is a figurative washing. Hence it is not objected that 'remaining limbs' if meant to include the head is incorrect because the head is not washed in ablution; if meant excluding it, then the mention of wiping is omitted.
[5] His saying: 'in the first scenario' is when he washed the feet first, put on the khuffs, then completed his ablution. In this scenario, at the time of putting on, there is only the washing of the feet, not complete purification, because he completed the ablution afterwards. However, it is true that he has a complete purification at the time of the later event, which is the duration of wearing, not the time of putting on.
[6] His saying: 'in the second scenario' is when he performed a sequential ablution and did not complete it, but rather put on...
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a super-commentary (hashiya) on Sharh al-Wiqayah. It addresses two key issues regarding wiping over khuffs and socks (jowrabayn). The first issue: the condition that socks be either mun'al (soled with leather on the bottom) or mujallad (covered with leather on top and bottom). The author explains that if socks meet this condition, wiping over them is unanimously permissible among the Hanafis (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad). However, if they are thick but not leather-lined, Abu Hanifa's earlier opinion prohibits wiping, while his two companions (Sahibayn) permit it if the socks are thick enough for continuous walking, thus resembling the khuff. The text states that Abu Hanifa reportedly retracted to their opinion shortly before his death, and that is the position upon which fatwa is given. The second issue concerns the condition of 'complete purification' (taharah tamah) at the time of the hadath (event that nullifies wudu). The commentary clarifies that the purification must be complete at the time of the event, not necessarily at the time of putting on the khuffs. Two illustrative cases are given: (1) washing both feet first, then putting on khuffs, then completing the rest of wudu, then having an event – here the purification is not complete at the time of putting on but is complete at the event; (2) washing the right foot, inserting it into the khuff, then washing the left foot and inserting – again, complete purification is achieved only after the left foot is washed. The commentary also notes that 'complete purification' excludes cases like tayammum or ablution with date-wine, and that the excused person (sahib al-'udhr) does not wipe outside the time.

Key Terms
طهر تام (taharah tamah): A complete ablution (wudu) that includes washing all required limbs in order.
وقت الحدث (waqt al-hadath): The time when an event that nullifies wudu occurs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first scenario of the passage (washing feet first, then putting on khuffs, then completing wudu), when is the purification considered complete?
A) At the time of putting on the khuffs.
B) At the time of the later hadath.
C) At the time of washing the feet.
D) Never complete.

Key Takeaway
The purification must be complete at the time of the hadath, not at the time of putting on the khuffs.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
10 min
Timing of Purification for Wiping over Khuffs
Hanafi ruling on timing of purification for wiping over khuffs, with Shafi'i dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for wiping over khuffs, apply it to two scenarios, and compare it with the Shafi'i position citing the key textual proof.

Core Idea
Purification is needed when the khuff prevents the event, not when it is worn.

The validity of wiping over khuffs depends on a specific condition regarding the timing of purification. Many assume that complete wudu is required at the moment you put on the khuffs, but the Hanafi school takes a different view. This lesson unpacks that condition by examining two practical scenarios and the key proof from the Sunnah, while also addressing the Shafi'i disagreement.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the condition that must be met for wiping to be valid: complete purification (tahr tam) at the time of the hadath (event that nullifies wudu), not necessarily at the time of wearing.
Note the key distinction: the condition is linked to the moment the khuff actually serves its purpose – preventing hadath from reaching the foot.
2
Analyse the two illustrative scenarios
Work through the two scenarios given in the text: (1) washing feet first, wearing khuffs, then completing wudu; (2) washing right foot, wearing right khuff, then washing left foot. In both, note that the person lacks complete purification at the time of wearing but has it before the hadath.
These scenarios show that the Hanafi position allows wiping even when wearing occurs before full wudu is complete.
3
Identify the Shafi'i position and its proof
Imam al-Shafi'i requires complete purification at the time of wearing. His proof is an analogy to other acts of worship that require purification at the start.
This is a clear ikhtilaf based on different interpretations of evidence.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response and its dalil
The Hanafi response relies on the hadith of al-Mughira, where the Prophet (PBUH) said 'I entered my feet into the khuffs while they were pure' – noting the feet's purity, not complete wudu. Additionally, the rationale is that the khuff prevents hadath, so the relevant moment is when the prevention takes effect (the hadath).
The explicit text (nass) overrides analogy (qiyas) in Hanafi usul, making the Hanafi proof stronger in their methodology.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical consequence: Hanafis permit wiping as long as complete purification exists at the time of hadath, even if not at wearing. Shafi'is would invalidate wiping in such cases. The Hanafi position is the mufta bihi within the school due to the explicit hadith and ease (taysir).
This difference affects daily practice, especially for those who wear khuffs before completing wudu or after partial washing.

Worn during complete purification at the time of the event. [1] He said: 'worn' is an attribute for each of the khuffs, jarmuqayn, and jorabayn. (2) He said: 'complete purification' excludes when a mutayammim or one who performed wudu with date wine wears them, for he does not wipe; and a person with an excuse during the excuse does not wipe outside the time. Thus in Jami' al-Rumuz and others. [3] He said: 'time of the event' is an adverb for 'complete'; meaning it is required that complete purification be at the subsequent event after wearing the khuffs, and it is not required that it be complete at the time of wearing and before the event as well. [4] He said: 'washed the remaining limbs' means washing is general including real and legal, so it includes wiping the head because it is legal washing; so it is not objected that if 'remaining limbs' includes the head it is incorrect because the head is not washed in wudu, and if it excludes the head the speech is devoid of mention of wiping. [0] He said: 'in the first scenario' which is when he washed the feet first, wore the khuffs, then completed his wudu; in this scenario at the time of wearing there is only washing of the feet, not complete purification, because he completed wudu afterwards; but indeed he is described as having complete purification at the subsequent event, which is the time of remaining wearing, not the time of its occurrence. [6] He said: 'in the second scenario' which is when he performed wudu in order and did not complete it, but wore the right khuff before completing it, then completed with washing the left; in this scenario he does not have complete purification at the time of wearing because wearing the right khuff was before its completion; but indeed he has complete purification at the time of the event, which is the time of remaining wearing. So in these two scenarios, wiping over the khuffs after the subsequent event is permissible due to the existence of remaining wearing upon complete purification, and its existence just before the subsequent event, even if the occurrence of wearing was not upon complete purification. And there is a dispute with al-Shafi'i, for he requires complete purification at the occurrence of wearing. Our proof: the khuff prevents the occurrence of the event from reaching the foot, so complete purification is considered at the time of prevention, which is the time of the event, not the time of wearing. This is supported by the hadith of al-Mughira reported by al-Bukhari and Muslim and others: he said 'I reached for the khuffs to remove them, but the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: Leave the khuffs, for I entered my feet into the khuffs while they were pure, then he wiped over them.' It is clear that what is considered is the purity of the foot that enters the khuff at the occurrence of wearing, not complete purification; otherwise it would have been said 'I entered my feet into the khuffs while I was pure' or similar. If you wish to see elaboration of the proofs of the madhhab, refer to al-Si'aya. [1] He said: 'time of the event' – there is that the time of the event is the time of nullification of purification, so how can it be the time of complete purification? The answer: what is meant is shortly before the time of the event. [2] He said: 'so it is known' etc.; it is a branching from what was mentioned in the two scenarios, and its direction is the author's choice of a different expression from the famous one. The summary: their saying 'if he wore them upon complete purification at the time of the event' apparently indicates the condition of complete purification at the beginning of wearing because the past tense indicates occurrence, while according to us it is not so.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage elucidates the Hanafi position on the condition of purification for wiping over khuffs (leather socks). The discussion begins by clarifying the phrase 'worn upon complete purification at the time of the event'. The commentator explains that 'complete purification' (tahr tam) excludes cases where the person performed tayammum or used date wine for wudu, as well as those with continuous excuses (sahib al-udhr) who may not wipe outside the time. The key condition is that the purification must be complete at the time of the subsequent hadath (event that nullifies wudu), not necessarily at the moment of wearing the khuffs. Two illustrative scenarios are given: (1) washing the feet first, wearing khuffs, then completing wudu; (2) washing the right foot, wearing the right khuff before finishing the left foot. In both cases, the person lacks complete purification at the time of wearing but has it at the time of hadath, making wiping permissible. The Hanafi school argues that the khuff prevents the hadath from reaching the foot, so the relevant time for purification is when the prevention takes effect, i.e., the hadath. This is supported by the hadith of al-Mughira, where the Prophet (PBUH) emphasized that he inserted his feet while they were pure, not that he was in a state of complete wudu. The Shafi'i position requires complete purification at the time of wearing, which the Hanafis reject based on textual evidence and legal reasoning (i'tibarat). The passage also addresses a potential objection regarding the phrase 'time of the event' being contradictory to the concept of purification, clarifying that it means immediately before the event. Overall, this is a nuanced argument defending the Hanafi view that the condition is tied to the time of hadath, not wearing.

Key Terms
طهر تام (tahr tam): Complete purification, i.e., the state of having valid wudu or ghusl that removes ritual impurity (hadath).
وقت الحدث (waqt al-hadath): The time when an event that nullifies wudu occurs (e.g., passing wind, urination).
ملبوسين (malbusayn): The two khuffs (or socks) being worn on the feet, as the condition 'worn upon complete purification' refers to their wearing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when must a person have complete purification (tahr tam) for wiping over khuffs to be valid?
A) At the time of wearing the khuffs.
B) At the time of the hadath (event nullifying wudu).
C) Both at the time of wearing and at the hadath.
D) At the time of performing wudu regardless of wearing.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi condition for wiping over khuffs is complete purification at the time of the hadath, not wearing, based on the hadith of al-Mughira.

The Condition for Wiping over Khuffs: Purity at the Time of Hadath
Refining the wording for the condition of wiping over khuffs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for wiping over khuffs, explain why the passive participle 'malbusayn' is preferred, and apply the condition to edge cases.

Core Idea
Wiping over khuffs is valid when the feet are pure at the time of hadath, not when the khuffs are put on.

This lesson refines a classic Hanafi ruling. The condition for wiping over leather socks (khuffs) is not that you wear them while in complete wudu, but that they remain on your feet when you are in a state of purity at the moment your wudu breaks. The commentator explains why the author's wording using the passive participle is superior.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed condition
Locate the two formulations: 'if he wears them in complete purity' vs. 'they are worn in complete purity at the time of hadath'. Note the difference between an action (wearing) and a state (being worn).
The passive participle 'malbusayn' denotes continuity.
2
Understand the Hanafi proof
Read the hadith of al-Mughirah where the Prophet said he inserted his feet while pure – not while in full wudu. This shows the relevant purity is of the feet, not the whole wudu. Also, the khuff prevents hadath from reaching the foot, so the condition applies at the time of prevention (hadath).
The 'time of hadath' means just before the nullifying event.
3
Apply to two scenarios
Consider the two cases: (a) wearing one khuff before completing wudu, then completing wudu and wearing the other; (b) wiping after a later hadath. In both, wiping is valid because the khuffs are present on pure feet at the time of hadath, even though not put on in full purity.
These scenarios illustrate the practical difference from the Shafi'i view.
4
Resolve the commentator's apparent contradiction
The commentator first calls the author's expression 'better' (ahsan) than the common one, then says the common expression is 'not correct'. Understand the two resolutions: either 'ahsan' is not comparative but merely affirms beauty, making the common expression ugly; or, if comparative, the common expression is so inferior it is deemed incorrect.
This shows linguistic precision in legal texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة / المسح على الخفين
لكنهما ملبوسان على طهارة كاملة وقت الحدث".
فَعُلِمَ" أن قوله : ملبوسين، أحسنُ من عبارتهم، وهي : إذا لَبِسَهُما على طهارة كاملة ؛ لأنَّ المراد الطهارة الكاملة وقت الحدث، وهذا الوقت هو زمان بقاء اللبس الخف في اليمنى قبل تمامه ثمَّ أمه بغسل اليسرى ، ففي هذه الصورة ليس له طهارة كاملة عند حدوث اللبس ؛ لأنّ لُبْسَ الخُفَّ في اليمنى كان قبل تمامها ، نعم له طهارة كاملة وقت الحدث، وهو زمان بقاء اللبس.
ففي هاتين الصورتين يجوز المسح على الخفين بعد الحدث اللاحق ؛ لوجود بقاء لبسهما على طهارة كاملة ، ووجودها قبيل الحدث اللاحق وإن لم يوجد حدوث اللبس على طهارة كاملة، وفيه خلافُ الشافعي الله فإنّه يشترط كمالُ الطهارة عند حدوثِ اللبس. ولنا : أنّ الخُفَّ يمنع حلول الحدث بالقدم، فيراعي كمال الطهارة وقت المنع ، وهو وقت الحدث لا وقت حدوث اللبس، ويؤيده حديث المغيرة له عند الشيخين البخاري ومسلم، وغيرهما : قال أهويت إلى الخفين لأنزعهما، فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم :
دع الخفين، فإنّي أدخلتُ القدمين الخفين وهما طاهرتان، فمسح عليهما ) (۱) فإنّه صريح في أنّ المعتبر هو طهارة القدم الذي يدخلُ في الخف عند حدوث اللبس لا الطهارة الكاملة، وإلا يقال : إني أدخلت القدمين الخفين، وأنا طاهر أو نحو ذلك ، وإن شئت الاطلاع على البسط في أدلّة المذهب، فارجع إلى «السعاية». [١]قوله : وقـت الحـدث ؛ فيه أنّ وقت الحدث وقت انتقاض الطهارة، فكيف يكون وقت الطهارة الكاملة.
وأجيب عنه : بأن المراد به قبيل وقت الحدث.
[٢] قوله : فعلم... الخ ؛ تفريع على ما ذكر في الصورتين، وتوجيهه لاختيار المصنف عبارة غير العبارة المشهورة، وحاصله أنّ قولهم : إذا لبسهما على طهارة كاملة وقت الحدث يدلّ بظاهره على اشتراط الطهارة الكاملة عند ابتداء اللبس ؛ لأنّ الفعل الماضي يدلّ على الحدوث مع أنه ليس كذلك عندنا.

(۱) في «صحيح البخاري (۱) : (۸۵) ، و سنن أبي داود»(١ : ٨٦)، وغيرها.

عمدة الرعاية على شرح الوقاية
لا زمان حدوثه ، فيصح أن يقال : هما ملبوسان على طهارة كاملة وقت الحدث ، ولا يصح أن يقال : لَبسَهُما على طهارة كاملة وقت الحدث
فإنّ المسح في الصورتين المذكورتين جائز مع عدم الطهارة الكاملة عند ابتداء اللبس، وإنّما الشرط عندنا هو كمالُ الطهارة عند الحدث، وهو زمان بقاء اللبس لا وقت ابتدائه.
فلذلك عدل المصنّف . عن تلك العبارة واختار صيغة اسم المفعول الدالة على الثباتِ والدوام ؛ فإنّه يصدقُ في الصورتين المذكورتين مما ليس له كمال الطهارة عند الابتداء أنهما ملبوسان على الطهارة الكاملة وقت الحدث، فيشملها كلامه.
فاعلم أن عبارة المصنّف أحسنُ من عبارتهم، وهاهنا أبحاث من وجوه : الأول : إن صدر كلام الشارح الله المفيد لأن عبارة المصنف له أحسن من عبارتهم، يدل على أن عبارتهم أيضاً صحيحة، وفيها أيضاً حسن ولطافة، لكن عبارة المصنف له أزيد منها حسناً ، وآخر كلامه وهو قوله : «ولا يصح أن يقال : لبسهما على طهارة كاملة وقت الحدث»، يدل على أن عبارتهم مما لا يصح فضلاً عن حسن ،
فبين كلاميه تناقض
والجواب عنه من وجهين :
أحدهما : أن يقال : إنَّ لفظ أحسن مجرَّد عن معنى التفضيل، فالغرض منه ليس إثبات زيادة الحسن، بل إثبات نفس الحسن، كما يقال : الأعم والأخص، ويراد به نفس العام والخاص.
فيدل كلامه حينئذ على أنّ في عبارة المصنّف حسناً، وفي عبارة الجمهور قبحاً،
وما يشمل على قبح فهو في حكم غير الصحيح ؛ فلذلك قال في آخر كلامه : «فلا يصح، فاندفع التعارض.
وثانيهما : إن سلمنا أن المراد بالأحسن هو ما فيه زيادة حسن، لكن لما كان غير الأحسن بمقابلة الأحسن في قوة القبيح وغير الصحيح عند العقلاء الحذاق أَطْلَقَ في آخر كلامه عدم الصحة فاندفع التناقض.
Book of Purification / Wiping over Khuffs: But they (the khuffs) are worn in a state of complete purity at the time of the event (hadath). So it is known that his statement 'worn' (malbusayn) is better than their expression which is: 'when he wears them while in a state of complete purity'; because what is meant is complete purity at the time of the event, and that time is the duration of wearing (remaining on the foot). Consider: [example of] the khuff on the right foot before completing (wudu) then he completes by washing the left foot – in this scenario, he does not have complete purity at the time of initiating wearing, because wearing the right khuff was before completing (wudu). Yes, he has complete purity at the time of the event, which is the duration of wearing. So in these two scenarios, wiping over the khuffs is permissible after the subsequent hadath, because the khuffs remain worn in a state of complete purity (at the time of hadath), and that state exists just before the subsequent hadath even if the initiation of wearing was not in a state of complete purity. In this matter there is a disagreement with al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, for he requires complete purity at the time of initiating wearing. Our proof: the khuff prevents the hadath from reaching the foot, so complete purity is considered at the time of prevention, which is the time of the hadath, not the time of initiating wearing. This is supported by the hadith of al-Mughirah, may Allah be pleased with him, recorded by al-Bukhari, Muslim, and others: He said: 'I reached for the khuffs to remove them, but the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: Leave the khuffs, for I inserted my feet into the khuffs while they (the feet) were pure, then he wiped over them.' This is explicit that what is considered is the purity of the foot that enters the khuff at the time of initiating wearing, not complete purity (of wudu). Otherwise, he would have said: 'I inserted my feet into the khuffs while I was in a state of purity (tahir)' or something similar. If you wish to see an elaboration of the proofs of the madhhab, refer to 'al-Si'ayah'. [1] His statement: 'at the time of the event' – there is a point: the time of the event is the time of nullification of purity, so how can it be the time of complete purity? The answer: what is meant is just before the time of the event. [2] His statement: 'So it is known...' etc.; this is a corollary of what was mentioned in the two scenarios, and its explanation for the author's choice of an expression different from the famous one. The gist is that their statement 'when he wears them while in a state of complete purity at the time of the event' apparently indicates the condition of complete purity at the initiation of wearing, because the past tense verb indicates occurrence, whereas it is not so in our (Hanafi) view. ... (1) From Sahih al-Bukhari (1:85) and Sunan Abi Dawud (1:86), etc. [PAGE_470] It is not the time of its initiation, so it is correct to say: 'they are worn in a state of complete purity at the time of the event', and it is not correct to say: 'he wore them in a state of complete purity at the time of the event'. Because wiping in the two mentioned scenarios is permissible despite the absence of complete purity at the initiation of wearing; indeed, the condition for us is complete purity at the time of the event, which is the duration of wearing, not the time of initiation. Therefore, the author (may Allah have mercy on him) departed from that expression and chose the passive participle form, which denotes constancy and continuity. For it holds true in the two mentioned scenarios where one does not have complete purity at initiation that they are 'worn in a state of complete purity at the time of the event', so his wording includes them. Know that the author's expression is better than their expression. Here there are investigations from several aspects: The first: The beginning of the commentator's statement (may Allah benefit us) 'because the author's expression is better than their expression' indicates that their expression is also correct, and it also has beauty and elegance, but the author's expression has more beauty. But the end of his statement: 'and it is not correct to say: he wore them in a state of complete purity at the time of the event' indicates that their expression is not correct, let alone beautiful. So there is a contradiction between his two statements. The answer is from two angles: One: It can be said that the word 'better' (ahsan) is stripped of the meaning of superiority; the purpose is not to prove increased beauty, but to prove beauty itself, just as one says 'al-a'am wa al-akhass' (the general and the specific) meaning the general and the specific themselves. So his statement then indicates that there is beauty in the author's expression and ugliness in the expression of the majority, and whatever contains ugliness is in the ruling of being incorrect; therefore he said in the end of his statement 'thus it is not correct', so the contradiction is resolved. Second: If we concede that 'better' means that which has increased beauty, then since that which is not 'better' in comparison to 'the better' is, in the estimation of intelligent, discerning people, tantamount to ugly and incorrect, he used the word 'incorrect' at the end of his statement, so the contradiction is resolved.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the wording used to state the condition for wiping over khuffs (leather socks) in the Hanafi madhhab. The author of the commentary (the hashiya) is refining the expression found in the sharh (explanation) of al-Wiqayah. The central issue is whether the condition is that the khuffs be worn in a state of complete ritual purity (i.e., after completing wudu) at the time of the hadath (event that nullifies wudu) or at the time of initially wearing them. The Hanafi position is that the condition is complete purity at the time of the hadath, not at the time of wearing. The commentator explains why the author of the original text (al-Wiqayah) chose the passive participle 'malbusayn' (worn) over the active verbal form 'labisahuma' (he wore them). The active form implies an action at a specific point in time, while the passive participle implies a continuous state. The two scenarios mentioned earlier (not fully reproduced here) involve cases where a person begins wearing a khuff before completing wudu, then completes wudu and wears the other; in these cases, the initiation of wearing was not in a state of complete purity, but the khuffs are continuously present on the feet at the time of the subsequent hadath while the person is in a state of complete purity. Thus, wiping is permissible. The Shafi'i school requires complete purity at the time of initiation, but the Hanafi proof is based on the hadith of al-Mughirah where the Prophet (peace be upon him) said he inserted his feet while they (the feet) were pure, not that he was in a state of complete wudu. This shows that the purity considered is that of the feet themselves, not the full wudu condition. The passage then addresses an apparent contradiction in the commentator's own statements: first calling the common expression 'better', then later saying it is 'not correct'. Two resolutions are offered: either 'better' is not used comparatively but simply to affirm beauty, making the common expression ugly and thus incorrect; or, if comparative, the lesser expression is so inferior as to be deemed invalid. This is a fine point of linguistic legal analysis, typical of the hashiyah genre.

Key Terms
ملبوسان (malbusayn): Passive participle (ism maf'ul) meaning 'worn' or 'being worn', indicating a continuous state of being on the feet.
طهارة كاملة (taharah kamilah): Complete ritual purity, specifically being in a state of wudu after washing all required limbs.
وقت الحدث (waqt al-hadath): The moment when an event that nullifies wudu occurs (e.g., passing wind, urination, etc.).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the correct condition for the validity of wiping over khuffs (mas-h 'ala al-khuffayn)?
A) The khuffs must be worn while the feet are completely pure at the time of wearing.
B) The khuffs must be worn in a state of complete ritual purity at the time of the hadath (event that nullifies wudu).
C) The khuffs must be worn while the feet are pure at the time of wearing, and the person must be in wudu at the time of hadath.
D) The khuffs must be worn only after completing wudu in full, and the person must remain in wudu until the hadath.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi condition for wiping over khuffs is that the khuffs are on the feet in a state of complete ritual purity at the time of the hadath, not at the time of wearing.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
6 min
The Condition of Complete Purity at the Time of the Event for Wiping over Khuffs
Supercommentary refining the linguistic expression of the condition for wiping over khuffs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for wiping over khuffs and explain why the passive participle 'malbūs' is the most precise expression for that condition.

Core Idea
The condition for wiping over khuffs is complete purity at the time of the event, not at wearing.

This supercommentary refines the wording used by jurists to describe the condition for wiping over khuffs. The author defends the matn's use of 'malbūsān' (worn) over 'labisahumā' (he wore them) because the verb implies a single act while the participle indicates a continuous state. Understanding this linguistic nuance is crucial for correctly applying the Hanafi ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two expressions being compared
The matn uses the passive participle 'malbūsān' (they are worn) while others use the active verb 'labisahumā' (he wore them). Notice which one the author prefers and why.
The verb 'labisa' focuses on the act of putting on; the participle 'malbūs' describes the ongoing state of being worn.
2
Understand the linguistic basis
The supercommentary explains that verbs (fi‘l) primarily indicate occurrence (ḥudūth), while nouns/participles (ism) indicate permanence (dawām). The author chose the participle to convey that the condition (purity) persists at the time of the event.
This is a key usul point: word choice affects legal meaning.
3
Analyze the contradiction and its resolution
The commentator first says the matn's expression is 'more beautiful' but later says the other expression is 'not correct'. Two resolutions: either 'more beautiful' is not comparative, or if it is, the inferior is effectively incorrect.
This shows the precision expected in legal writing.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider: A person begins wudu, puts on khuffs after washing some limbs but before completing wudu, then completes wudu. Later, after a hadath, they wipe over khuffs. According to Hanafi, this is valid because at the time of the hadath they had complete purity, regardless of the state at wearing.
The condition is tied to the event, not the initial wearing.
5
Refine the expression
The correct phrasing is: 'the khuffs are worn (malbūsān) in a state of complete purity at the time of the event.' This includes cases where purity was incomplete at the moment of wearing but complete at the event.
The passive participle ensures the condition is met.

لأن الفعل دال على الحدوث، والاسم دال على الدوام والاستمرار. الثاني : إن الذي بنى عليه الشارح الله الفرق بين العبارتين من دلالة الفعل على الحدوثِ والاسم على الدوام والاستمرار غير صحيح، ، فكثيراً ما يجيء الفعلُ للاستمرار والاسم لنفس الثبوت، إلا أن يقال : غرضُه أنّ الأصل في الفعل الدلالة على الحدوث، وفي الاسم الاستمرار ، فلا يضر استعمال أحدهما في الآخر.

‘Umdat al-Ri‘āyah ʿalā Sharḥ al-Wiqāyah
...not the time of its occurrence. So it is correct to say: they are two worn [items] in a state of complete purity at the time of the event, and it is not correct to say: he wore them in a state of complete purity at the time of the event. For wiping is permissible in the two mentioned cases despite the absence of complete purity at the beginning of wearing; indeed the condition according to us is complete purity at the time of the event, which is the time of the persistence of wearing, not the time of its beginning. Therefore the author changed that expression and chose the form of the passive participle indicating stability and permanence; for it applies to the two mentioned cases where one does not have complete purity at the beginning, that they are worn in complete purity at the time of the event, so his statement includes them. Know that the author’s expression is more beautiful than theirs, and here are investigations in several aspects: First: The beginning of the commentator’s statement, ‘because the author’s expression is more beautiful than theirs’, indicates that their expression is also correct, and in it there is also beauty and subtlety, but the author’s expression is more beautiful than theirs. And the end of his statement, which is his saying: ‘and it is not correct to say: he wore them in a state of complete purity at the time of the event’, indicates that their expression is not correct, let alone beautiful. So there is a contradiction between his two statements. The answer to it is from two aspects: One of them: It is said that the word ‘more beautiful’ is stripped of the meaning of comparison; the purpose is not to establish an increase in beauty, but rather to establish beauty itself, as it is said ‘the more general and the more specific’, and what is meant is the general and the specific themselves. Then his statement indicates that the author’s expression has beauty, and the expression of the majority has ugliness, and whatever includes ugliness is in the ruling of being incorrect; therefore he said at the end of his statement: ‘so it is not correct’, so the contradiction is repelled. The second: If we concede that what is meant by ‘more beautiful’ is that which has an increase in beauty, yet since what is not more beautiful in comparison to the more beautiful has the force of ugly and incorrect in the view of discerning intellects, he declared in his final statement the lack of correctness, so the contradiction is repelled.

[PAGE_471]
The Book of Purity / Wiping over the Khuffs
473
...because the verb indicates occurrence, and the noun indicates permanence and continuity. Second: That on which the commentator based the difference between the two expressions, namely the verb indicating occurrence and the noun indicating permanence and continuity, is not correct; for frequently the verb comes for continuity and the noun for mere existence, unless it is said that his purpose is that the original in the verb is to indicate occurrence, and in the noun it is continuity, so using one for the other does not harm. However, a third objection then arises: that the fact that the original in the verb is occurrence is conceded, but that the original in the noun is continuity is denied; for they explicitly stated that the noun and the nominal sentence are originally placed for existence irrespective of continuity or its absence, and continuity is only understood from a contextual or verbal indication. (1) Fourth: They explicitly stated that if someone swears he will not wear this garment while he is wearing it and does not remove it immediately, he breaks his oath – this is stated in Al-Hidāyah (5:104). This indicates that the verb is used more broadly for both occurrence and continuation. Similarly, His saying: “Then do not sit with the wrongdoing people after the reminder” (al-An‘ām 6:68) means do not continue sitting with them. Unless it is said that the commentator does not deny the use of the verb for continuation, but his point is that the original in the verb is its use for occurrence; if that is otherwise, it is due to a contextual requirement or an external indication. Fifth: They explicitly stated that the active and passive participles are also for occurrence. Its answer: Although the original is that, their use for continuation is widespread and frequent compared to the use of the verb for continuation, and therefore the commentator ruled the author’s expression as better. Sixth: The statement of the jurists ‘at the time of the event’ is connected to their statement ‘complete’, not to their statement ‘when he wore them’; so that the meaning is: wiping over the khuffs is permissible if he wore them in a state of purity, the completeness of which is at the time of the event. Thus its implication and the implication of the author’s expression are one. And in this context there are other investigations spread out in Al-Sa‘āyah.
(1) Al-Hidāyah (5:104).
(2) Al-An‘ām: from āyah 68.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This is a critical analysis from a super-commentary (hashiya) on ‘Umdat al-Ri‘āyah, itself a commentary on Sharḥ al-Wiqāyah. The author refines the explanation of the condition for wiping over khuffs in wuḍū’. The core issue is the proper wording to express that the condition is complete purity (ṭahārah kāmilah) at the time of the event (ḥadath), not at the initial wearing of the khuffs. The author (of the matn, Al-Wiqāyah) uses the passive participle ‘malbūsān’ (worn) to convey a state of being that persists, whereas others use the active verb ‘labisahumā’ (he wore them) which implies a one-time act. The commentator (the original sharh) is criticized for saying first that the matn’s expression is ‘more beautiful’ (aḥsan) but later saying the other expression is ‘not correct’. The hashia resolves this apparent contradiction in two ways: either ‘aḥsan’ is not comparative but simply means ‘good’ (thus the other is bad and thus incorrect), or if it is comparative, then the non-superior is effectively incorrect. The hashia then engages in linguistic analysis: verb indicates occurrence (ḥudūth), noun indicates permanence (dawām). Objections are raised that verbs can also indicate continuity (e.g., oath examples from Al-Hidāyah and Qur’an 6:68), and that participles are also originally for occurrence. The hashia responds that the original use of verbs is for occurrence, but they are used for continuity by context; participles, though originally for occurrence, are more commonly used for continuity than verbs. Finally, it notes that the phrase ‘at the time of the event’ in the jurists’ statement qualifies ‘complete’, not ‘when he wore them’, making the condition the completeness of purity at the moment of the event. The overall teaching is that the Hanafi condition for wiping over khuffs is complete purity at the time of the event (ḥadath), and the matn’s wording using the passive participle is the most precise.

Key Terms
الفعل (al-fi‘l): In this passage, 'al-fi‘l' refers to the linguistic verb form (like 'labisa') which primarily indicates an occurrence or event in time, as opposed to a state.
الاسم (al-ism): Here 'al-ism' refers to the participle (ism al-maf‘ūl) 'malbūs', which indicates a state that is stable and continuous, not tied to a specific moment of occurrence.
كمال الطهارة (kamāl al-ṭahārah): Complete purity, meaning that one has a valid wuḍū’ or ghusl and retains it until the moment of the event (ḥadath) that would break it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did the author of the matn choose the passive participle 'malbūsān' (worn) instead of the active verb 'labisahumā' (he wore them)?
A) Because the passive participle is grammatically more correct in Arabic.
B) Because the passive participle indicates a state of permanence and continuity, which accurately reflects the condition of purity at the time of the event.
C) Because the active verb is considered incorrect by majority of Hanafi scholars.
D) Because the passive participle is easier to understand for laypeople.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi condition for wiping over khuffs is complete purity at the time of the event, best expressed by the passive participle 'malbūsān' to convey the ongoing state.

The Linguistic Resolution of Purity Timing for Khuff Wiping
Defending the wording 'wiping is valid if put on in complete purity at the time of event'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the phrase 'at the time of the event' modifies 'complete' and not 'when he put them on' in the condition for wiping over khuffs, and apply this condition to practical scenarios.

Core Idea
The purity for khuff wiping must be complete at the moment of the hadath, not at the moment of wearing.

When do you need to have complete wudu for wiping over khuffs? At the moment you put them on or at the moment your wudu breaks? This lesson resolves a subtle linguistic dispute between the author of Al-Hidayah and its commentator, revealing that the key phrase 'time of the event' attaches to 'complete purity', not to 'wearing'. Understanding this nuance ensures correct application of a common legal concession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the competing formulations
The author of the matn and the commentator (sharh) use different wordings for the condition of wiping over khuffs. One links 'complete purity' to the act of wearing, the other to the time of the event (hadath). Recognize these two formulations.
The matn says 'completion is at the time of the event', while the sharh says 'complete purity at the time of wearing'.
2
Analyze the linguistic argument
The commentator initially argued that verbs (like 'wipes') indicate occurrence while nouns indicate continuity. This is critiqued. Understand the objections: verbs can imply continuity (e.g., Quran 6:68), and nouns indicate existence, not necessarily continuity.
The critique shows the commentator's general linguistic claim is oversimplified.
3
Evaluate the sixth point
The passage concludes with a key resolution: the phrase 'time of the event' is attached to 'complete', not to 'when he put them on'. This means the completeness of purity is assessed at the moment the hadath occurs, not at the moment of wearing.
This reconciles both formulations: the purity at wearing must be such that it would be complete if an event occurred.
4
Derive the legal condition
From the sixth point, the legal condition for wiping over khuffs is: wiping is valid if the person put on the khuffs while in a state of purity, and that purity was complete at the moment the hadath (event that breaks wudu) occurred. This typically means the hadath happens after wearing while still in full wudu.
If a hadath occurs before wearing, the condition is not met.

(1) «الهداية»(5 : 104). (2) الأنعام : من الآية68 .
Because the verb indicates occurrence, and the noun indicates permanence and continuity. Second: The distinction that the commentator built between the two expressions based on the verb indicating occurrence and the noun indicating permanence and continuity is incorrect, for often the verb is used for continuity and the noun for mere existence, unless it is said: his purpose is that the original usage of the verb is to indicate occurrence, and of the noun is continuity, so using one for the other does no harm. Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the wording in the matn regarding the condition for wiping over khuffs. The author of the sharh had argued that the verb 'yamsahu' indicates occurrence and the noun indicates continuity, to justify a particular formulation. The commentator here refutes that distinction, showing that verbs can indicate continuity and nouns can indicate mere existence. He then resolves that the original usage of the verb is for occurrence, but the noun's original usage is for existence, not continuity. The sixth point provides the key conclusion: the phrase 'at the time of the event' (waqt al-hadath) modifies the word 'complete' (kamilah), not the phrase 'when he put them on' (idha labisahuma). This means that the purity must be complete at the moment of the event (e.g., after breaking wudu), not necessarily at the moment of putting on the khuffs. This preserves the meaning of both the author and the sharh's expressions.

Key Terms
الفعل (al-fi'l): The verb, which in Arabic grammar primarily indicates occurrence (حدوث) at a specific time, but can also indicate continuity.
الاسم (al-ism): The noun or noun phrase, which primarily indicates a fixed attribute (ثبوت) without regard to time, but can imply continuity through context.
الحدوث (al-huduth): Occurrence or taking place at a specific moment; the primary meaning of the verb.
الثبوت (al-thubut): Existence or being established; the primary meaning of the noun.
الاستمرار (al-istimrar): Continuity or duration; a secondary meaning derived from context for both verb and noun.

Key Takeaway
The precise condition for wiping over khuffs is that the purity be complete at the time of the hadath, not at the time of wearing.

Wiping over Turban, Cap, and Mittens is Not Permitted
Ruling that wiping over items besides khuffs is not allowed, with a rebuttal of opposing views

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school prohibits wiping over head coverings and gloves as a substitute for washing, and evaluate the counter-argument of Imam Ahmad and others.

Core Idea
Wiping in place of washing is an exception to qiyas and cannot be extended beyond what the texts report.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi position on wiping over items that cover the head, face, or hands during wudu. The school holds that only khuffs and bandages are permissible substitutes; all other coverings, such as turbans, caps, veils, and mittens, do not suffice. This ruling stems from the principle that wiping as a substitute is a concession (rukhsah) limited to explicit textual evidence. We will also explore the dissenting view of Imam Ahmad and others.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the items mentioned and the ruling that wiping over them does not fulfil the obligation of washing the face or hands.
The passage lists turban, cap, veil, and mittens.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that Imam Ahmad, al-Awza'i, and Ishaq allow wiping over the turban.
Their view is based on hadiths where the Prophet wiped his turban.
3
Analyze the opponent's proof
Examine the hadith of 'Amr ibn Umayyah and Bilal that the Prophet wiped his turban.
These hadiths are found in Bukhari and Muslim.
4
Examine the Hanafi response
Understand the Hanafi refutation: the hadiths actually show wiping the forelock along with the turban, so the head is still wiped.
Thus, they do not prove exclusive wiping over the turban.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Note the practical difference: in the Hanafi school, wudu is invalid if only the turban is wiped; in the opposing view, it is valid.
The Hanafi view is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : لا على عمامة ; أي لا يجوز المسح على عمامة – وهو بكسر العين - : ما يلفه الرجال على رؤوسهم، ولا على قلنسوة - بفتح القاف واللام، وسكون النون، وضم السين المهملة، وفتح الواو - ما يستر به الرأس ، يقال له بالفارسية : كلاه ، فلو لم يمسح شيئاً من الرأس مسح على عِمامةٍ أو فَلَنْسُوةٍ لم يجزئه ذلك. وكذا لا يجوز المسحُ مقامَ غسل الوجهِ على بُرقع – وهو بضم الباء الموحدة ، وسكون الراء المهملة، ثم قاف مفتوحة، ثم عين مهملة - : خرقة تستر بهما النساء وجوههن وأعينهن. وكذا لا يجوز المسح مقام غسل الكفِّين على قفازين – بضم القاف، وتشديد الفاء بعد الألف زاي معجمة ، والقفاز ما يلبس في الكف الصائد لدفع مخالب الطيور، وقد يكون من كرباس يلبس لدفع البرد ونحوه، ويقال له بالفارسية : دستانه. والوجه في ذلك كله أن ثبوت المسح وقيامه مقام الغُسل على خلاف القياس ، فيقتصر على ما ورد به، ولم يرد في الشرع الاكتفاء بالمسح على هذه الأشياء، وقيامه مقامَ الغُسل، ولا دخل للرأي فيه حتى يثبت جوازه بالقياس على مسح الخفين، وهذا كله قول الجمهور. واختار أحمد والأوزاعي وإسحاق رحمهم الله جواز المسح على العمامة مستنداً بأنه ثبت عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم برواية عمرو بن أمية الضمري عند البخاري (٢)، وبلال عند مسلم وغيره. وأجاب الجمهور عنه بأنه لا دلالة لما رووه على عدم مسح الرأس، والاكتفاء بالعمامة، بل في بعضها عند مسلم وغيره تصريح بأنه «مسح على ناصيته وعمامته»(۳)، بخلاف مسح الخفين، فإنّ كثيراً من الروايات دلّت على الاكتفاء به ، وإن شئت الاطلاع على زيادة التفصيل في هذا البحث فعليك بـ«السعاية».
His statement: 'Not on a turban, and not on a cap, and not on a veil, and not on mittens.' (i.e., wiping is not allowed on a turban – with kasra on the 'ayn – which is what men wrap on their heads, nor on a cap – with fatha on qaf and lam, sukun on noona, damm on the important seen, and fatha on waw – which covers the head, called in Persian 'kulah'; so if he did not wipe any part of the head and wiped on a turban or cap, that would not suffice him. Similarly, wiping is not allowed in place of washing the face on a burqu' – with damm on the ba, sukun on the ra, fatha on qaf, then 'ain with sukun – which is a cloth with which women cover their faces and eyes. Similarly, wiping is not allowed in place of washing the hands on qifazan – with damm on qaf, tashdid on fa, after alif a zay with i'jam, and qifaz is what the hunter wears on his hand to repel the claws of birds, and it may be made of coarse cloth worn to repel cold and the like, called in Persian 'dastanah'. The rationale for all of this is that the establishment of wiping in place of washing is contrary to analogy, so it is confined to what is reported, and there is no report in the Shariah of deeming wiping on these things sufficient and it standing in place of washing; and there is no room for personal opinion in it, so that its permissibility could be proven by analogy to wiping over khuffs. This is all the view of the majority. Ahmad, al-Awzai, and Ishaq (may Allah have mercy on them) chose the permissibility of wiping over the turban, based on what is reported from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) through the narration of Amr ibn Umayyah al-Damari in al-Bukhari (2), and through Bilal in Muslim and others. The majority responded to him that what they narrated does not indicate not wiping the head and being satisfied with the turban; rather, in some of them, in Muslim and others, there is explicit mention that 'he wiped over his forelock and turban' (3), unlike wiping over khuffs, for many narrations indicate deeming it sufficient. And if you wish to see further detail on this research, refer to al-Sa'ayah.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with an inter-madhhab dispute. It first states the ruling of the Hanafi school (and the majority of scholars) that it is not permissible to wipe over a turban, cap, veil (burqu'), or mittens (qifaz) as a substitute for washing the face or hands in wudu. The reason given is that wiping as a substitute is an exception to the general principle (qiyas), so it is restricted to what is explicitly mentioned in the revealed texts. The Hanafi position is that only khuffs and similar leather socks, and bandages (jabirah), have been reported as permissible. The passage then presents the dissenting view of Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal, al-Awza'i, and Ishaq ibn Rahwayh, who allow wiping over the turban based on hadiths where the Prophet (peace be upon him) wiped over his turban. The majority response is that those hadiths do not prove that the turban suffices without wiping the head; instead, in some versions, the Prophet also wiped his forelock, indicating that wiping the head was still done. This contrasts with wiping over khuffs, where many hadiths explicitly state that the Prophet wiped over them without touching his feet. Thus, the Hanafi position remains the relied-upon view.

Key Terms
عمامة (imamah): A turban worn by men on the head.
قلنسوة (qalansuwah): A cap that covers the head.
برقع (burqu'): A veil covering the face and eyes, worn by women.
قفاز (qifaz): Mittens or gloves worn to protect hands, used by hunters or for cold.
مسح (mash): Wiping; in this context, a substitute for washing in wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because the turban is not made of leather.
D) Because the Prophet never wiped his turban.

Key Takeaway
Wiping over head coverings or gloves as a substitute for washing the face or hands is invalid in the Hanafi school because such wiping is not established by definitive texts and cannot be analogized from khuffs.

Ruling on Wiping Over Turban, Cap, Burqa, and Gloves in Wudu
Inter-madhhab dispute with ruling and proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wiping over headgear and gloves and explain the legal reasoning based on the principle that wiping in place of washing is restricted to transmitted evidence.

Core Idea
Wiping over a turban does not substitute for wiping the head — the exception is strictly textual, not analogical.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling that wiping over a turban, cap, burqa, or gloves does not suffice for the required washing of the head or hands in wudu. The passage explains the legal principle that such dispensations are contrary to qiyas and thus limited to textual proof. We also explore the inter-madhhab dispute with Ahmad and others who permitted wiping over the turban based on hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the items and the ruling
The passage lists four items: turban (عمامة), cap (قلنسوة), burqa (برقع), and gloves (قفازين). The ruling is that wiping over them does not suffice in place of the required washing.
The ruling applies to both head coverings and hand coverings.
2
Understand the legal reasoning
The permissibility of wiping as a substitute for washing is contrary to qiyas (analogy), so it is restricted to what has been transmitted in the Sharia. No transmitted evidence supports these items.
This is the principle of 'al-iqtisar 'ala al-warid'.
3
Examine the opposing view
Ahmad, al-Awza'i, and Ishaq permit wiping over the turban based on hadith from 'Amr ibn Umayyah and Bilal where the Prophet wiped his turban.
These are ahadith in Bukhari and Muslim.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The majority responds that those hadith do not prove omitting the head; some versions explicitly mention wiping the forelock and turban together.
This distinguishes the case from khuffs, where clear narrations indicate sufficing with khuffs alone.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafi wudu is invalid if only the turban is wiped; the opponent's wudu would be valid.
This affects the validity of salah performed with such wudu.

لا على عمامة، وقَلَنْسُوة، ويُرْقُع، وقُفَّازَيْن) (۱) : القفاز : ما يلبس على الكف ليكف عنها مخلب الصقر ونحوه. قوله : لا على عمامة؛ أي لا يجوز المسح على عمامة – وهو بكسر العين - : ما يلفه الرجال على رؤوسهم، ولا على قلنسوة - بفتح القاف واللام، وسكون النون، وضم السين المهملة، وفتح الواو - ما يستر به الرأس، يقال له بالفارسية : كلاه، فلو لم يمسح شيئاً من الرأس مسح على عمامة أو قلنسوة لم يجزئه ذلك. وكذا لا يجوز المسح مقام غسل الوجه على برقع – وهو بضم الباء الموحدة، وسكون الراء المهملة، ثم قاف مفتوحة، ثم عين مهملة - : خرقة تستر بهما النساء وجوههن وأعينهن. وكذا لا يجوز المسح مقام غسل الكفين على قفازين – بضم القاف، وتشديد الفاء بعد الألف زاي معجمة، والقفاز ما يلبس في الكف الصائد لدفع مخالب الطيور، وقد يكون من كرباس يلبس لدفع البرد ونحوه، ويقال له بالفارسية : دستانه. والوجه في ذلك كله أن ثبوت المسح وقيامه مقام الغسل على خلاف القياس، فيقتصر على ما ورد به، ولم يرد في الشرع الاكتفاء بالمسح على هذه الأشياء، وقيامه مقام الغسل، ولا دخل للرأي فيه حتى يثبت جوازه بالقياس على مسح الخفين، وهذا كله قول الجمهور. واختار أحمد والأوزاعي وإسحاق جواز المسح على العمامة مستنداً بأنه ثبت عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم برواية عمرو بن أمية الضمري عند البخاري، وبلال عند مسلم وغيره. وأجاب الجمهور عنه بأنه لا دلالة لما رووه على عدم مسح الرأس، والاكتفاء بالعمامة، بل في بعضها عند مسلم وغيره تصريح بأنه «مسح على ناصيته وعمامته»، بخلاف مسح الخفين، فإنّ كثيراً من الروايات دلّت على الاكتفاء به، وإن شئت الاطلاع على زيادة التفصيل في هذا البحث فعليك بـ«السعاية».
Not upon a turban, nor a cap, nor a veil, nor gloves. (1) The glove: what is worn on the hand to repel the talon of a falcon and the like. He said: 'Not upon a turban' means that wiping upon a turban is not permissible—it is with a kasrah on the 'ayn—what men wrap around their heads, nor upon a cap—with a fatha on the qaf and lam, sukun on the nun, dammah on the seen, and fatha on the waw—what covers the head, called in Persian 'kulah'. So if one does not wipe any part of the head but wipes on a turban or cap, it does not suffice. Likewise, it is not permissible to wipe in place of washing the face upon a veil—with dammah on the ba, sukun on the ra, fatha on the qaf, then 'ayn—a cloth by which women cover their faces and eyes. Likewise, it is not permissible to wipe in place of washing the hands upon gloves—with dammah on the qaf, shaddah on the fa after alif, zay with nuqtah, and the glove is what is worn on the hand of a hunter to repel the talons of birds, and it may be made of coarse cloth worn to repel cold and the like, called in Persian 'dastaneh'. The reasoning for all of this is that the establishment of wiping and its standing in place of washing is contrary to analogy, so it is restricted to what is transmitted. And there is no transmitted permission in the Sharia for sufficing with wiping on these things and their standing in place of washing, and there is no role for opinion in it such that its permissibility could be established by analogy to wiping over khuffs. And all of this is the saying of the majority. And Ahmad, al-Awza'i, and Ishaq chose the permissibility of wiping over the turban, relying on what was established from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) via the narration of 'Amr ibn Umayyah al-Damari in al-Bukhari, and Bilal in Muslim and others. The majority responded that what they narrated does not indicate the omission of wiping the head and sufficing with the turban; rather, in some of them, according to Muslim and others, it explicitly states that 'he wiped over his forelock and turban', unlike wiping over khuffs, for many narrations indicate sufficing with it. And if you wish to see more detail on this research, refer to 'al-Si'ayah'.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the ruling that wiping over a turban, cap, burqa, or gloves in place of the required washing (head or hands) is not valid in the Hanafi school. The text first defines each item, then gives the ruling that such wiping does not suffice, and explains the legal reasoning: wiping in substitution for washing is an exception to qiyas (analogical reasoning), so it is confined strictly to what the Sharia has transmitted. Since there is no transmitted evidence allowing these items to substitute for washing, wiping over them is invalid. The text then presents a known inter-madhhab dispute: Imam Ahmad, al-Awza'i, and Ishaq permitted wiping over a turban based on hadith from Amr ibn Umayyah and Bilal. The Hanafi response is that those hadith do not prove that wiping over the turban alone suffices; in fact, in some narrations the Prophet wiped both his forelock and turban. This differs from the case of khuffs, where clear narrations show that wiping only over the khuffs suffices. Thus the Hanafi position remains mufta bihi.

Key Terms
عمامة (imamah): A cloth wrapped around the head by men; wiping over it does not substitute for wiping the head
قلنسوة (qalansuwah): A cap covering the head, called 'kulah' in Persian; wiping over it does not suffice
برقع (burqu'): A cloth covering women's face and eyes; wiping over it in place of washing face does not suffice
قفازين (qufazayn): Gloves worn to protect hands, either by hunters or from cold; wiping over them does not substitute for washing hands

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because the turban is not a common head covering
C) Because the act of wiping in place of washing is contrary to qiyas and there is no transmitted proof for the turban
D) Because the hadith about the Prophet wiping his turban is weak

Key Takeaway
Wiping over a turban, cap, burqa, or gloves does not suffice in wudu because such dispensations are limited to clear textual evidence, which is absent for these items.

The Obligatory Amount for Wiping Over Khuffs
Specifies the minimum obligatory amount for wiping over khuffs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the obligatory amount for wiping over khuffs and distinguish between the fard (measure) and the sunnah (using three fingers).

Core Idea
The fard is the area of three fingers, not the act of using three fingers.

Many assume that wiping over khuffs must be done with exactly three fingers. The Hanafi school clarifies that the obligation is the area covered—a measure equal to the width of three fingers of the hand. Using three fingers is a sunnah, not a requirement. The evidence comes from the Prophet's wiping described as 'lines', indicating he used his fingers, not his palm. This lesson unpacks the ruling and the subtle distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? Wiping over khuffs. What is the hukm level for the amount? Fard (obligatory).
The fard is a minimum requirement, not a recommendation.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites actions of the Prophet (peace be upon him) reported by al-Hasan al-Basri and al-Mughirah ibn Shu'bah, describing the wiping as 'lines' (khutut). This shows he used his fingers.
The dalil is a hadith from the Musannaf of Ibn Abi Shaybah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The description 'lines' implies the wiping was done with the fingers, not the whole hand. Therefore, the minimum area to be wiped is the width of three fingers (the width of a typical finger line).
The obligation is the measure (qadr), not the specific fingers.
4
Note the ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school
Al-Karkhi considered three toes of the foot as the measure, while the majority (including al-Hidayah) adopt three fingers of the hand, considering the instrument of wiping.
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is three fingers of the hand.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Al-Hidayah and others correct the hand fingers as the preferred view. Thus, the operative ruling for fatwa is the measure of three fingers of the hand.
The authority weight is zahir al-riwayah.

وفرضُهُ قَدْرُ ثلاث أصابع اليد، فإِنَّ مَسْحَ رَسولُ الله صلى الله عليه وسلم كان خُطُوطاً فَعُلِمَ أَنَّها بالأصابع دون الكف. قوله: قدر؛ أي مقدار ثلاث أصابع اليد فيه إشارة إلى أن المسح بثلاث أصابع اليد بعينها ليس بفرض إنّما هو سنة، والفرضُ إنما هو مقداره، واعتبار أصابع اليد هو اختيار كثير من الفقهاء، وهو الذي صححه في «الهداية» وغيره، اعتباراً لآلة المسح. واعتبر الكرخي له ثلاث أصابع من أصابع الرجل اعتباراً باعتبارها في مسائل خرق الخف كما سيأتي في موضعه. قوله : كان خطوطاً؛ كما يعلم ما رواه ابن أبي شيبة في «مصنفه» عن الحسن البصري قال : السنة أن يمسح على الخفين خطوطاً. وروي أيضاً عن المغيرة بن شعبة قال : رأيت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بال ثم جاء ومسح.
And its obligatory amount is the measure of three fingers of the hand, for the wiping of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) was lines, so it is known that it was with the fingers, not the palm. He said: 'qadr' meaning the measure of three fingers of the hand. In this is an indication that wiping with specifically three fingers is not an obligation; rather, it is a sunnah, and the obligation is only its measure. And considering the fingers of the hand is the choice of many jurists, and it is what was corrected in al-Hidayah and others, considering the instrument of wiping. And al-Karkhi considered for it three fingers from the toes, considering their consideration in issues of holes in the khuff as will come in its place. He said: 'was khututan' (was lines); as is known from what Ibn Abi Shaybah narrated in his Musannaf from al-Hasan al-Basri who said: 'The sunnah is to wipe over the khuffs with lines.' And it was also narrated from al-Mughirah ibn Shu'bah who said: 'I saw the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) relieve himself, then he came and wiped.'
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage clarifies the obligatory amount for wiping over khuffs (leather socks). The base text states that the fardh (obligatory minimum) is the measure of three fingers of the hand. The commentary explains that the fardh is the quantity (measure), not the specific act of using three fingers, which is a sunnah. The evidence is that the Prophet's wiping was described as 'lines' (khututan), indicating he used his fingers (not the whole palm). Hence the minimum area that must be covered is equivalent to the width of three fingers. The majority of Hanafi scholars (such as the author of al-Hidayah) adopt the hand fingers as the reference because they are the instrument of wiping. However, al-Karkhi considered the three fingers of the foot as the measure, analogizing from rulings on holes in the khuff. The passage then cites the hadith through al-Hasan al-Basri and al-Mughirah ibn Shu'bah to support the 'lines' method.

Key Terms
فرض (fard): The obligatory minimum area of the khuff that must be wiped; it is the measure (قدر) of three fingers, not the act of using three fingers
خطوط (khutut): Lines; describing the Prophet's wiping as lines indicates he used his fingers, not the whole palm
قدر (qadr): Measure/quantity; the fardh is the measure of three fingers, not necessarily the exact fingers

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Wiping the entire foot
C) Wiping an area equal to three fingers of the hand in measure
D) Wiping with the whole palm

Key Takeaway
The fard for wiping over khuffs is the area equal to three fingers of the hand; using three fingers is sunnah, not a condition.

The Obligatory Measure for Wiping Over Khuff
Determining the obligatory amount of wiping over khuff

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the obligatory measure for wiping over khuff in the Hanafi school and distinguish it from the sunnah action, citing the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
The fard is the measure of three fingers, not the exact number of fingers used.

Wiping over khuff (leather socks) is a concession in wudu, but what exactly makes it valid? The classical Hanafi texts specify a minimum measure. This lesson unpacks that measure from the proof of the Prophet's practice and clarifies a common misunderstanding between obligation (fard) and sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is wiping over khuff, and the ruling is fard (obligatory). The specific fard is the measure of three fingers of the hand.
The commentator emphasizes that using exactly three fingers is sunnah, not fard.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith of al-Mughira ibn Shu'ba, where the Prophet wiped his khuff with his fingers, leaving lines.
Other hadith (Jabir via Tabarani) also support finger-wiping.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith shows wiping with fingers, and the minimum plural (three) establishes the obligatory measure as three fingers – the certain minimum.
The commentator uses the principle of al-mutayaqqan (the certain) to fix the measure.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam al-Karkhi considered the measure as three fingers from the foot, but the majority (mufta bihi) adopt the hand measure.
The hand is the instrument of wiping, so it logically determines the measure.

Book of Purity / Wiping over Khuff. And its obligation is the measure of three fingers of the hand. It is worn on the palm; to ward off from it the claw of the hawk and the like. And its obligation is the measure of three fingers of the hand, for indeed the wiping of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) was lines. So it is known that it is with the fingers, not the palm. [1] His statement: 'to ward off' is known, and its subject is a pronoun referring to the wearer, or it is passive; i.e., to prevent from it, i.e., from the palm; 'mikhlab' (with kasra of the meem, sukoon of the kha, and fatha of the laam) is a substitute for the subject on the second interpretation, and an object on the first. He said in 'Muntaha al-Arab': 'khalb' (with kasra) is a nail, and 'mikhlab' (with kasra) is the claw of birds of prey, meaning the talons of predatory birds. 'Al-Saqr' (with fatha of the sad) - he said in 'Muntaha al-Arab': it is a bird of prey like the falcon and hawk and others. End. [2] His statement: 'and the like'; it is either with kasra, conjoined to 'al-saqr', and its pronoun refers to it, or it is conjoined to 'al-mikhlab', or with damm, also conjoined to it according to one of the two interpretations we mentioned previously. [3] His statement: 'measure'; i.e., the quantity of three fingers of the hand. In it is an indication that wiping with the three fingers themselves is not an obligation but rather a sunnah, and the obligation is only its measure. And considering the fingers of the hand is the choice of many jurists, and it is what he declared sound in 'al-Hidayah' and others, considering the instrument of wiping. And al-Karkhi considered for it three fingers from the toes, considering them in issues of a tear in the khuff as will come in its place. [4] His statement: 'was lines'; as is known from what Ibn Abi Shayba narrated in his 'Musannaf' from al-Hasan al-Basri who said: The sunnah is to wipe over the khuff in lines. Also from al-Mughira ibn Shu'ba who said: I saw the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) relieve himself, then he came and performed wudu and wiped over his khuff, and he placed his right hand on his right khuff and his left hand on his left khuff, then he wiped the upper part of them in one wipe, until it was as if I was looking at the fingers of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) on the khuff. And Ibn Majah narrated from Jabir (may Allah be pleased with him): 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) gestured with his hand like this from the tips of the fingers to the top of the shin,' and its chain is weak. And al-Tabarani narrated in his 'al-Mu'jam al-Awsat' from Jabir: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) passed by a man performing wudu and he was washing his khuff, so he nudged him with his hand and said: 'We were only commanded to wipe like this,' and he extended his hand from the front of the khuff to the top of the shin once, and spread his fingers.' Mentioned by al-Ayni in 'al-Binaya'. [11] His statement: 'And what is more than...' etc.; this statement has interpretations: The first: that its purpose is to explain the consideration of three fingers, not more, and to refute the objection that what is established in the hadith is wiping with the fingers, and there is no restriction to three in it. Its conclusion: when it is known that wiping is with the fingers, if four or five fingers are considered, the additional wiping would be with used water, so it is not considered, and therefore it does not enter into the obligated. And its clarification: that what is more than three is either with new water other than that for the three, or with water used by the three. On the first, it necessitates multiple wipes, and on the second, it necessitates using used water. But there is a point: that the water of the hand, as long as it remains on the limb, does not take the ruling of being used until it separates from the limb, as has passed in its place. And the best way to determine three is that since it is established that the wiping of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) was with the fingers, and the minimum plural is three, we say: we assume the certain amount, not more than that.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the obligation of wiping over khuff (leather socks) during wudu, specifically regarding the required amount. The author first states the ruling from the matn: 'Its obligation is the measure of three fingers of the hand.' He then explains that the khuff is worn on the hand to protect it from the claws of hawks, etc. The measure is derived from the Prophet's practice of wiping in lines with his fingers, not the whole palm. The commentator clarifies that 'wiping with three fingers themselves is not fard but sunnah; the fard is the measure (quantity) of three fingers.' He notes that considering the fingers of the hand is the choice of many Hanafi jurists, as affirmed in Al-Hidayah. There is an intra-Hanafi difference: Imam al-Karkhi considered the measure to be three fingers from the toes, based on analogies in cases of torn khuff. The commentator then provides hadith evidence: a narration from al-Mughira that the Prophet wiped his khuff with a single wipe using both hands, and the lines indicate finger-wiping. Two other hadith from Jabir are cited but one is weak (Ibn Majah) and the other from Tabarani supports wiping from front to shin with spread fingers. The commentator addresses a potential objection: if wiping is established with fingers, why limit to three? He explains that if more than three fingers are used, either it would require using new water (making multiple wipes) or using water already used by the first three fingers. But he notes that water on the hand does not become 'used' until it leaves the limb, so the better argument is that the Prophet's wiping was with fingers, and the minimum plural is three; thus we take the certain minimum (mutayaqqan) and do not add beyond it.

Key Terms
خف (khuff): A leather sock worn over the foot that covers the ankle and is worn inside shoes; it is permissible to wipe over it during wudu.
مسح (mas'h): Wiping over the khuff with wet hands as a substitute for washing the feet in wudu.
أصابع (asabi'): The fingers of the hand (or foot); here it specifies the fingers as the instrument for wiping.
خطوط (khutut): Lines; indicating that the Prophet's wipe left traces in lines, showing it was done with fingers not the whole palm.
ماء مستعمل (ma' musta'mal): Water that has been used in a valid act of worship and is no longer pure (according to some views).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the obligatory amount for wiping over khuff?
A) The whole hand
B) The measure of three fingers of the hand
C) Three fingers of the foot
D) The entire palm

Key Takeaway
The fard in wiping over khuff is to wipe an area equivalent to three fingers of the hand.

Obligatory Amount of Wiping Over Khuffs: Three Fingers and the Evidence
Explaining why the obligatory wiping covers only three fingers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the obligatory amount of wiping over khuffs (three fingers), identify the primary hadith evidence, and explain the rationale using the principle that the minimum of plural is three.

Core Idea
The fard of wiping over khuffs is limited to three fingers; excess is not considered due to used water or multiple wipes.

This lesson unpacks a key ruling in Hanafi fiqh: the obligatory amount for wiping over leather socks (khuffs) in wudu is three fingers. The source text, a commentary on al-Hidayah, cites prophetic practice and offers three interpretations to solidify this limit. By the end, you'll understand not only the ruling but also the reasoning that prevents the obligation from extending further.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Determine the act being ruled upon (wiping over khuffs) and the specific hukm: the fard (obligatory) amount is three fingers of the hand.
2
Locate the primary dalil
Find the hadith evidence cited: the narration in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah describing the Prophet (peace be upon him) wiping his khuffs with his hand, emphasizing the fingers.
The hadith is ahad (single-narrator) but accepted in practice.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Understand how the hadith proves the three-finger ruling: the mention of 'fingers' indicates the hand, and the minimum of the plural (three) is taken as the certain amount, following the usul principle that the minimum of a plural is three.
This is a key Hanafi usul application.
4
Examine the three interpretations
Review the three rebuttals (mahamil) that address potential objections: wiping with spread fingers, extending to the shin, etc. Each argues that any excess beyond three fingers involves either used water (invalid) or multiple wipes (not part of the sunnah).
These interpretations prevent the obligatory amount from expanding.
5
Understand the absence of additional conditions
Note that the commentator states that no extra conditions like intention (niyyah), order (tartib), or continuity (muwalat) are required for wiping over khuffs, unlike tayammum, due to lack of evidence.
This differentiates khuff wiping from other substitute purifications.

الثاني : أن يكون دفعاً لما يقال : قد مَرَّ أن المسنون هو المسح بالأصابع المنفرجة ، فيلزم الزيادة على مقدار الثلاث لانتشار الماء من الأصابع الثلاثة في فرجاتها ، وحاصله : أنّ ما زاد على الثلاث إنّما هو مستعمل لا ماء جديد ، فلا اعتبار له ، فبقي مقدار الثلاث.
والثالث : أن يكون دفعاً لما يقال من أنّ المسنون هو مد الأصابع إلى الساق، فتلزمُ الزيادة على مقدار الثلاث، وحاصله : أنّ ما زاد على مقداره بمده إنمـا هـو بـمـاء مستعمل، فلا اعتبار له، وفي المقام أبحاث سؤالاً وجواباً، فلتطلب من «السعاية».
