Conditions for Wiping Over a Wound Bandage: Hardship and Ability
Commentary on conditions for wiping over bandages

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for permissible wiping over a wound bandage in the Hanafi school and evaluate the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding ability with assistance.

Core Idea
Wiping over a bandage is only a concession when direct washing harms the wound or the bandage cannot be undone alone.

This lesson examines the legal conditions for wiping over a wound bandage (al-mash 'ala al-'isaba). The Hanafi school permits this concession to avoid hardship, but the precise conditions vary: some focus on the inability to undo the bandage without help, while others emphasize the harm that would result from removing it. We will explore both criteria and the intra-madhhab disagreement on whether ability with another's assistance nullifies the concession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two criteria
The passage presents two approaches: (a) inability to undo the bandage without assistance, and (b) harm to the wound from removal. Determine which one is primary in the text.
Abu Hanifa considers inability alone as sufficient; harm is a separate condition.
2
Trace Abu Hanifa's position on ability
According to Abu Hanifa, ability with the help of others is not considered true ability. If one cannot manage alone, the concession applies even if help is available.
This is based on the principle of hardship (al-mashaqqa).
3
Note the Sahibayn's objection
The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) likely consider ability with assistance as sufficient, so the concession would not apply if help is available. This disagreement parallels the earlier issue of cracks (shuqaq).
The text says 'fihi khilafun lahuma'.
4
Connect to the underlying legal principle
The ruling is based on the maxim 'al-mashaqqa tajlib al-taysir' (hardship brings ease). The concession is limited to the extent of necessity.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider a patient who can ask a nurse to change the bandage. According to Abu Hanifa, wiping is still allowed because the patient cannot do it alone. According to the Sahibayn, wiping is not allowed because help is available.
This demonstrates the practical difference (thamara al-khilaf).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند البعض : إن أمكنه شَدُّ العصابة بلا إعانة أحد لا يجوز عليها المسح، وإن لم يُمْكِنْهُ ذلك يجوز.
إن كان حل العصابة وغَسْلُ ما تحتها يضرُّ الجراحة ، جازَ المسح عليها، وإلا فلا ، وكذا الحكم في كلِّ خرقة جاوزت موضع القرحة. وإن كان حلُّ العصابة لا يَضُرُّه ، لكنَّ نَزْعَها عن موضع الجراحة يضره يحلها، ويغسلُ ما تحتها إلا موضع الجراحة ، ثُمَّ يشدها، ويمسح موضع الجراحة.
قوله : إن أمكنه ؛ أي إن كان يمكنُ منه أن يحلّ العصابة فيمسح على الخرقة التي تحتها، ثم يشدها من غير إعانة أحد لا يجوز له المسح على العصابة ؛ لعدم الحرج حينئذ في حلها، وإمكان المسح على الخرقة.
وإن لم يمكنه ذلك جاز المسحُ عليه، إذ لا معتبر بالقدرة على الشيء بإعانة الغير عند أبي حنيفة ، فعدم إمكانه حلّها وشدّها بدون الإعانة يثبت العجز عن المسح على الخرقة، فتقومُ العِصابة مقامها ، وفيه خلافٌ لهما على نحو ما مرَّ في مسألة الشقوق.
قوله : وقال بعضهم ... الخ ؛ حاصلُ هذا القول أنّ العبرة في هذا الباب للضرر والحرج ؛ فإن كان حَلُّ العصابة ومسحُ الخرقة لا يضر الجراحة، لم يجز المسح على العصابة، وإن كان ذلك مضراً بها جاز المسحُ عليها ؛ لأنَّ التكليف بحسب الوسع ، ولو التصقت العصابة بالمحل بحيث يعسر نزعها جاز المسحُ أيضاً، ولو كان بعد البرء، لكن حينئذ يمسحُ على الملتصق ويغسلُ ما قدر على غسله من الجوانب.
قوله : وكذا الحكم ؛ أي مثلُ الحكم في عصابة المفتصد ؛ فإذا كانت زائدة عن موضع الجراحة فإن ضره الحل والغَسل مسح الكلّ ، وإلا يغسل ما حول الجراحة ويمسح عليها لا على الخرقة ما لم يضره مسحها، فإن ضر يمسح على الخرقة التي عليها، ويغسلُ حواليها، وما تحت الخِرقة الزائدة ؛ لأنَّ الثابت بالضرورة يتقدر بقدرها. كذا فصله في «البحر الرائق» وغيرها.
وعامة المشايخ على جواز مسح عصابة المفتصد، وأما الموضعُ الظَّاهِرِ من اليد ما بين العقدتين من العصابة ، فالأصح أنه يكفيه المسح، إذ لو غسل تبتل العصابة، فربما تنفذ البلة إلى موضع الفصد.
ويشترط الاستيعاب في مسح الجبيرة والعصابة في رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة ، وهو المذكور في «الأسرار» وعند البعض : يكفي الأكثر.
قوله : فالأصح ح (۳) ؛ أشار به إلى أن فيه خلافاً ، فقد قيل : يجب غسله كما في «الخلاصة».
قوله : في رواية الحَسَن له ؛ كذا نسبه إليه قاضي خان له في «فتاواه» ، ونسب صاحب «الهداية» إليه القول الثاني.
قوله : يكفي الأكثر ؛ هذا هو الذي صححه صاحب «الكنز» في «الكافي» ، وعلله بأنه لو اشترط الاستيعاب لاحتيج إلى الاستقصاء في إيصال البلل إلى جميع أجزاء الخرقة ونحوها، فيؤدي إلى نفوذ البلة إلى الجراحة.
And according to some: If he can tie the bandage without the assistance of anyone, wiping over it is not permissible, and if he cannot do that, it is permissible. If undoing the bandage and washing what is under it would harm the wound, then wiping over it is permissible; otherwise no. And the same ruling applies to every cloth that exceeds the wound area. And if undoing the bandage does not harm him, but removing it from the wound area harms him, he undoes it, washes what is under it except the wound area, then ties it and wipes over the wound area. His statement: 'If he can' - meaning if he is able to undo the bandage and wipe over the cloth under it, then tie it without anyone's assistance, it is not permissible for him to wipe over the bandage due to the absence of hardship in undoing it and the possibility of wiping over the cloth. And if he cannot do that, wiping over it is permissible, because ability through another's assistance is not considered according to Abu Hanifa. So his inability to undo and tie it without assistance establishes his inability to wipe over the cloth, so the bandage takes its place. There is a disagreement with them (the two disciples) in a manner similar to what passed in the issue of cracks. His statement: 'And some said': The gist of this statement is that the consideration in this chapter is for harm and hardship. If undoing the bandage and wiping the cloth does not harm the wound, wiping over the bandage is not permissible, and if it is harmful, wiping over it is permissible because obligation is according to capacity. And if the bandage adheres to the wound such that removing it is difficult, wiping is also permissible even after healing, but then he wipes over the adhered part and washes what he is able to wash from the sides. His statement: 'And the same ruling' - meaning like the ruling for the bandage of a cupped patient. If it extends beyond the wound area, and if the undoing and washing harm him, he wipes the entire bandage; otherwise, he washes around the wound and wipes over the wound itself, not over the cloth unless wiping it harms him; if it harms, he wipes over the cloth on it, washes around it and what is under the extra cloth, because what is established by necessity is limited to its extent. Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and others have detailed it thus. And the generality of mashayikh (scholars) are upon the permissibility of wiping over the bandage of a cupped patient. As for the apparent part of the hand between the two knots of the bandage, the sahih (correct opinion) is that wiping suffices him, because if he washes, the bandage becomes wet, and perhaps the wetness penetrates to the cupping site. And isti'ab (covering entirely) is required in wiping over the splint and bandage in the narration of Hasan from Abu Hanifa, and it is mentioned in Al-Asrar. And according to some, the majority (al-akthar) suffices. His statement: 'The sahih': He pointed out that there is disagreement, for it was said: Washing it is obligatory as in Al-Khulasa. His statement: 'In the narration of Hasan': Qadi Khan attributed it to him in his Fatawa, and the author of Al-Hidaya attributed the second opinion to him. His statement: 'The majority suffices': This is what the author of Al-Kanz authenticated in Al-Kafi, and he argued that if isti'ab were required, it would necessitate thoroughness in delivering moisture to all parts of the cloth and the like, leading to the penetration of moisture to the wound.
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya is a commentary (sharh) on the rulings for wiping over a wound bandage (al-mash 'ala al-'isabah). The passage discusses several conditions and opinions within the Hanafi school.

First, the text presents a ruling: if a person can tie the bandage without anyone's help, then wiping over it is not allowed; but if he cannot, it is allowed. The commentator explains that Abu Hanifa does not consider ability with the help of others as a valid ability—true inability is when one cannot do it alone. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree on this point, as seen earlier in the issue of cracks.

Second, another opinion states that the criterion is harm and hardship: if removing the bandage and washing under it harms the wound, wiping over it is allowed; otherwise not. Obligation is according to capacity. If the bandage is stuck and removal is difficult, wiping is allowed even after healing, but then one wipes only over the adhered part and washes the rest.

Third, the same ruling applies to an excess cloth that goes beyond the wound area. If removing the entire cloth causes harm, one wipes over all; if not, one washes around the wound and wipes only the wound, not the cloth—unless wiping the cloth itself causes harm, then one wipes over the cloth. This is because a concession by necessity is limited to its extent.

Fourth, concerning the bandage of a cupped patient, the majority of scholars permit wiping over it. For the visible part of the hand between the knots, the correct opinion is that wiping suffices, as washing would soak the bandage and possibly reach the cupping site.

Finally, there is a dispute about whether one must cover the entire bandage with wetness (isti'ab) or only the majority. Hasan ibn Ziyad narrated from Abu Hanifa that isti'ab is required. However, later scholars like the author of Al-Kanz in Al-Kafi prefer the view that covering the majority suffices, because requiring full coverage would necessitate thorough wetting, which might harm the wound.

Key Terms
العصابة (al-'isaba): a bandage or wrapping used to cover a wound
الحرج (al-haraj): hardship or difficulty

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling if a person can have someone else help him remove and retie his wound bandage?
A) He must wash the wound and cannot wipe over the bandage.
B) He may still wipe over the bandage because ability with help is not considered true ability.
C) He may wipe only if the wound would be harmed by washing.
D) He must remove the bandage and wash, regardless of harm.

Key Takeaway
Wiping over a wound bandage is a concession allowed only when there is a genuine hardship or harm, and according to Abu Hanifa, even the availability of help does not remove the concession.

The Required Coverage When Wiping Over a Bandage: Isti'ab or Majority?
Commentary on the required coverage for wiping over bandages

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two positions on the required coverage when wiping over a wound bandage (isti'ab vs al-akthar) and explain the reasoning behind the preferred view.

Core Idea
The later Hanafi position prefers wiping the majority of the bandage to avoid harmful moisture reaching the wound.

This lesson examines a key dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the extent of the bandage that must be wiped. The early narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad requires covering the entire bandage (isti'ab), while many later scholars hold that wiping the majority (al-akthar) is sufficient. Understanding this dispute is essential for applying the concession without causing additional harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
The text mentions two views: (1) isti'ab (covering the entire bandage) in the narration of Hasan from Abu Hanifa, and (2) that the majority (al-akthar) suffices, authenticated by the author of Al-Kanz.
These are both within the Hanafi school.
2
Trace the narration of Hasan
Hasan ibn Ziyad transmitted from Abu Hanifa that isti'ab is required. This is mentioned in Al-Asrar and attributed by Qadi Khan.
But the author of Al-Hidaya attributed the opposite to Abu Hanifa.
3
Understand the reasoning for al-akthar
The author of Al-Kafi argues that requiring full coverage would demand thorough moistening, which could cause water to penetrate to the wound and cause harm.
This is a practical application of the principle of harm avoidance.
4
Note the disagreement on the apparent part
The text also mentions the visible part of the hand between the knots: the correct opinion is that wiping suffices because washing would wet the bandage and possibly harm the wound.
This is consistent with the majority view.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that wiping the majority is sufficient, as authenticated by later scholars.
This is the position for fatwa today.

وعند البعض : إن أمكنه شَدُّ العصابة بلا إعانة أحد لا يجوز عليها المسح، وإن لم يُمْكِنْهُ ذلك يجوز.
إن كان حل العصابة وغَسْلُ ما تحتها يضرُّ الجراحة ، جازَ المسح عليها، وإلا فلا ، وكذا الحكم في كلِّ خرقة جاوزت موضع القرحة. وإن كان حلُّ العصابة لا يَضُرُّه ، لكنَّ نَزْعَها عن موضع الجراحة يضره يحلها، ويغسلُ ما تحتها إلا موضع الجراحة ، ثُمَّ يشدها، ويمسح موضع الجراحة.
قوله : إن أمكنه ؛ أي إن كان يمكنُ منه أن يحلّ العصابة فيمسح على الخرقة التي تحتها، ثم يشدها من غير إعانة أحد لا يجوز له المسح على العصابة ؛ لعدم الحرج حينئذ في حلها، وإمكان المسح على الخرقة.
وإن لم يمكنه ذلك جاز المسحُ عليه، إذ لا معتبر بالقدرة على الشيء بإعانة الغير عند أبي حنيفة ، فعدم إمكانه حلّها وشدّها بدون الإعانة يثبت العجز عن المسح على الخرقة، فتقومُ العِصابة مقامها ، وفيه خلافٌ لهما على نحو ما مرَّ في مسألة الشقوق.
قوله : وقال بعضهم ... الخ ؛ حاصلُ هذا القول أنّ العبرة في هذا الباب للضرر والحرج ؛ فإن كان حَلُّ العصابة ومسحُ الخرقة لا يضر الجراحة، لم يجز المسح على العصابة، وإن كان ذلك مضراً بها جاز المسحُ عليها ؛ لأنَّ التكليف بحسب الوسع ، ولو التصقت العصابة بالمحل بحيث يعسر نزعها جاز المسحُ أيضاً، ولو كان بعد البرء، لكن حينئذ يمسحُ على الملتصق ويغسلُ ما قدر على غسله من الجوانب.
قوله : وكذا الحكم ؛ أي مثلُ الحكم في عصابة المفتصد ؛ فإذا كانت زائدة عن موضع الجراحة فإن ضره الحل والغَسل مسح الكلّ ، وإلا يغسل ما حول الجراحة ويمسح عليها لا على الخرقة ما لم يضره مسحها، فإن ضر يمسح على الخرقة التي عليها، ويغسلُ حواليها، وما تحت الخِرقة الزائدة ؛ لأنَّ الثابت بالضرورة يتقدر بقدرها. كذا فصله في «البحر الرائق» وغيرها.
وعامة المشايخ على جواز مسح عصابة المفتصد، وأما الموضعُ الظَّاهِرِ من اليد ما بين العقدتين من العصابة ، فالأصح أنه يكفيه المسح، إذ لو غسل تبتل العصابة، فربما تنفذ البلة إلى موضع الفصد.
ويشترط الاستيعاب في مسح الجبيرة والعصابة في رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة ، وهو المذكور في «الأسرار» وعند البعض : يكفي الأكثر.
قوله : فالأصح ح (۳) ؛ أشار به إلى أن فيه خلافاً ، فقد قيل : يجب غسله كما في «الخلاصة».

قوله : يكفي الأكثر ؛ هذا هو الذي صححه صاحب «الكنز» في «الكافي» ، وعلله بأنه لو اشترط الاستيعاب لاحتيج إلى الاستقصاء في إيصال البلل إلى جميع أجزاء الخرقة ونحوها، فيؤدي إلى نفوذ البلة إلى الجراحة.
And according to some: If he can tie the bandage without the assistance of anyone, wiping over it is not permissible, and if he cannot do that, it is permissible. If undoing the bandage and washing what is under it would harm the wound, then wiping over it is permissible; otherwise no. And the same ruling applies to every cloth that exceeds the wound area. And if undoing the bandage does not harm him, but removing it from the wound area harms him, he undoes it, washes what is under it except the wound area, then ties it and wipes over the wound area. His statement: 'If he can' - meaning if he is able to undo the bandage and wipe over the cloth under it, then tie it without anyone's assistance, it is not permissible for him to wipe over the bandage due to the absence of hardship in undoing it and the possibility of wiping over the cloth. And if he cannot do that, wiping over it is permissible, because ability through another's assistance is not considered according to Abu Hanifa. So his inability to undo and tie it without assistance establishes his inability to wipe over the cloth, so the bandage takes its place. There is a disagreement with them (the two disciples) in a manner similar to what passed in the issue of cracks. His statement: 'And some said': The gist of this statement is that the consideration in this chapter is for harm and hardship. If undoing the bandage and wiping the cloth does not harm the wound, wiping over the bandage is not permissible, and if it is harmful, wiping over it is permissible because obligation is according to capacity. And if the bandage adheres to the wound such that removing it is difficult, wiping is also permissible even after healing, but then he wipes over the adhered part and washes what he is able to wash from the sides. His statement: 'And the same ruling' - meaning like the ruling for the bandage of a cupped patient. If it extends beyond the wound area, and if the undoing and washing harm him, he wipes the entire bandage; otherwise, he washes around the wound and wipes over the wound itself, not over the cloth unless wiping it harms him; if it harms, he wipes over the cloth on it, washes around it and what is under the extra cloth, because what is established by necessity is limited to its extent. Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and others have detailed it thus. And the generality of mashayikh (scholars) are upon the permissibility of wiping over the bandage of a cupped patient. As for the apparent part of the hand between the two knots of the bandage, the sahih (correct opinion) is that wiping suffices him, because if he washes, the bandage becomes wet, and perhaps the wetness penetrates to the cupping site. And isti'ab (covering entirely) is required in wiping over the splint and bandage in the narration of Hasan from Abu Hanifa, and it is mentioned in Al-Asrar. And according to some, the majority (al-akthar) suffices. His statement: 'The sahih': He pointed out that there is disagreement, for it was said: Washing it is obligatory as in Al-Khulasa. His statement: 'In the narration of Hasan': Qadi Khan attributed it to him in his Fatawa, and the author of Al-Hidaya attributed the second opinion to him. His statement: 'The majority suffices': This is what the author of Al-Kanz authenticated in Al-Kafi, and he argued that if isti'ab were required, it would necessitate thoroughness in delivering moisture to all parts of the cloth and the like, leading to the penetration of moisture to the wound.

الأكثر (al-akthar): the majority (more than half) of the bandage's surface

Rulings on Wiping over Bandages (Jaba'ir)
State rulings on wiping over bandages for wounds

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for wiping over bandages according to the Hanafi school and identify when the wipe is valid or must be repeated.

Core Idea
Wiping over bandages requires covering the majority; one wipe suffices, and removal without re-wiping may invalidate.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi rulings on wiping over bandages (jaba'ir) and wound dressings (ʿiṣāba). While the basic principle is that the bandage takes the place of the skin, the jurists made specific allowances to prevent harm to the wound. We will cover the conditions of the wipe, the dispute over covering the entire bandage, and the effects of removing and replacing the bandage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main rulings
Read the passage and list the distinct rulings: permissibility, condition of istī'āb, wiping over exposed area, removal/replacement, and tathlīth.
2
Understand the dispute on istī'āb
Focus on the sentence requiring istī'āb in the narration of al-Hasan and the alternative view that majority suffices. Note the relied-upon position.
This is an intra-Hanafi disagreement; both views are valid.
3
Analyze removal and replacement rulings
Examine the three scenarios: wiping then removing without replacing, wiping then removing and replacing, and the bandage falling off and being replaced with another.
The key is whether the bandage is replaced or not.
4
Note the negation of tathlīth
Read the final sentence: tathlīth is not required; one wipe suffices. Contrast with wiping over khuffs.
This is a clear difference from khuffs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعامة المشايخ على جواز مسح عصابة المقتصد، وأما الموضع الظاهر من اليد ما بين العقدتين من العصابة، فالأصح أنه يكفيه المسح، إذ لو غسل تبتل العصابة، فربما تنفذ البلة إلى موضع [1] ويشترط الاستيعاب في مسح الجبيرة والعصابة في رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة، وهو المذكور في «الأسرار» وعند البعض: يكفي الأكثر. [2] [3] واذا مسح، ثم نزعها، ثم أعادها، فعليه أن يعيد المسح، وإن لم يعد أجزأه. وإذا سقطت عنها فبدلها بأخرى، فالأحسن إعادة المسح، وإن لم يعد [1] ولا يشترط تثليث مسح الجبائر، بل يكفيه مرة واحدة، وهو الأصح. [2] [3]
And the majority of scholars agree on the permissibility of wiping over the bandage of the phlebotomist. As for the apparent part of the hand that is between the two knots of the bandage, the correct view is that wiping suffices for it, because if he were to wash it, the bandage would become wet, and perhaps the moisture would penetrate to the wound. And istī'āb (covering the entire bandage) is required in wiping over the splint and bandage in the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa, and it is mentioned in al-Asrar. And according to some, the majority suffices. And if he wipes, then removes it, then puts it back, he must repeat the wiping, and if he does not put it back, it suffices. And if it falls off and he replaces it with another, it is better to repeat the wiping, and if he does not repeat, it suffices. And tathlīth (wiping three times) is not required for wiping over splints; rather, one time suffices, and that is the correct view.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage presents several rulings on wiping over bandages (جَبِيرَة) and bandages (عِصَابَة) applied to wounds. First, it addresses the area of the hand that is not covered by the bandage: the correct position (الأصح) is that wiping over the bandage suffices for that part too, because washing it would wet the bandage and risk reaching the wound, causing harm. Second, it discusses the condition of 'istī'āb' (covering the entire bandage during wiping). According to a narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa, it is required to cover the entire bandage, as mentioned in 'al-Asrar' by al-Dabusi. However, some scholars consider it sufficient to wipe the majority (الأكثر), which is the preferred view (صححه صاحب الكنز). Third, it rules on removing the bandage: if one wipes, then removes the bandage and then puts it back on (without healing), he must repeat the wipe; but if he does not put it back, the first wipe suffices because the excuse (wound) remains. If the bandage falls off and he replaces it with another, it is better to repeat the wipe, but if he does not, it is still valid. Fourth, unlike wiping over khuffs, wiping over bandages does not require three wipes (تثليث); one wipe suffices, which is the relied-upon position. All these rulings are based on the principle of preventing hardship (حرج) and allowing ease for the wounded person.

Key Terms
مسح (mash): Wiping lightly with wet hand over a limb or covering, as a substitute for washing.
جبيرة (jabirah): A splint or cast made of wood or cloth used to set a broken bone; here used generally for any wound covering.
استيعاب (istī'āb): Covering the entire surface of the bandage/splint during wiping.
تثليث (tathlīth): Wiping three times; specifically, the requirement to wipe three times for bandages is negated here.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a person wipes over their bandage and then the bandage falls off and they put on a new one, what is the ruling?
A) He must repeat the wipe.
B) It is better to repeat the wipe, but if he does not, it suffices.
C) He must wash the wound.
D) The first wipe is invalid and he must start over.

Key Takeaway
Wiping over bandages requires covering the majority, one wipe suffices, and re-wiping after replacement is recommended but not obligatory—all to prevent harm to the wound.

Key Differences Between Wiping Over Khuff and Jabirah
Explaining multiple legal distinctions between wiping over khuff and jabirah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state at least five key differences between wiping over khuff and jabirah and explain the legal basis for each difference, including the role of hardship (haraj).

Core Idea
Wiping over bandage (jabirah) differs fundamentally from wiping over leather socks (khuff) in duration, conditions, and scope due to differing proofs and the principle of hardship.

In Hanafi fiqh, wiping (mash) over certain coverings is a concession that replaces washing. However, the rules for wiping over leather socks (khuff) and over a bandage or splint (jabirah) are not identical. This lesson, drawn from 'Umdat al-Ri‘ayah, explores the key distinctions that a student must know to apply each ruling correctly. Understanding these differences is essential for giving accurate fatwa in purification matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two acts
First, recognize that both 'mash al-khuff' and 'mash al-jabirah' are forms of wiping that substitute for washing the limbs in wudu/ghusl. The khuff covers the foot; the jabirah covers a wound or fracture.
Each is based on different proofs: khuff on Sunnah (hadiths), jabirah on hadith of ‘Ali.
2
Recognize the general principles
Khuff wiping has strict conditions: requires complete purity before wearing, limited duration (24h/72h), etc. Jabirah wiping is more lenient due to hardship (haraj) – it is not limited in time, does not require prior purity, etc.
The principle of ‘haraj’ (hardship) removes restrictions for jabirah.
3
List the first four differences from the text
From the text: 1) Jabirah does not require prior wudu; khuff does. 2) Jabirah is not timed; khuff is. 3) If jabirah falls off before healing, previous wiping remains; if khuff is removed, wiping is invalid. 4) If jabirah falls after healing, only that spot is washed; removing one khuff requires washing both feet.
The commentator lists these as the first four in the passage.
4
Understand the ta‘lil (reasoning)
For each difference, ask why it exists. The text explains: for khuff, the proof (hadith) specified conditions; for jabirah, the absence of such specification and the presence of hardship justify leniency. The ta‘lil often refers to the nature of the covering: khuff is a barrier (mani‘) that when removed exposes the foot to impurity; jabirah is a substitute for washing the wound.
Example: khuff removal allows hadath to reach the foot; jabirah removal does not invalidate because it was a substitute, like shaving hair after wudu.
5
Apply to a scenario
Given a scenario (e.g., a person with a broken leg bandaged from the knee down, and another person wearing khuffs), determine the rulings: for the bandaged person, wiping is valid without prior wudu, for any duration, and if the bandage falls off before healing, no re-wiping needed; for the khuff-wearer, must have wudu before wearing, time-limited, and removal invalidates wiping.
This step integrates the differences into practical fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة المسح على الخفين أجزاء.
واذا مسح ، ثُمَّ نزعَها ، ثُمَّ أعادها ، فعليه أن يعيد المسح ، وإن لم يعد أجزأه". وإذا سقطت عنها فبدلها بأخرى، فالأحسنُ إعادة المسح، وإن لم يعد ا ولا يشترط تثليث مسح الجبائر، بل يكفيه مرة واحدة، وهو الأصح". ويجب أن يعلم أن مسح الجبيرة يُخالِفُ مسحَ الحُفٌ في : وفي «العناية»: «الفرق بينه وبين مسح الرأس والخف حيث لا يشترط فيهما الأكثر : إن مسح الرأس شرع بالكتاب والباء دخلت على المحل فأوجب تبعيضه ، والمسحُ على الخفين إن كان بالكتاب كان حكمه حكم المعطوف عليه، وإن كان بالسنة فهي أوجبت مسح البعض، فأما المسحُ على الجبائر فإنّما يثبت بحديث علي ، وليس فيه ما ينبئ عن البعض إلا أنَّ القليل سقط اعتباره للحرج).
[1]قوله : أجزأه ؛ أي كفاه ؛ لأنّ ناقض المسح إنّما هو سقوطه ونزعُهُ عن بُرء، لا بدونه ؛ لقيام العذر الموجب للعجز، فمجرد النزع لا يوجب إعادة المسح ولا غسل ما تحتها.
(۲)

1. أنه يجوز على حدث. ٢. ولا يُقدَّر له مُدَّة.
. وإذا سقطت لا عن بُرْء لا يبطل.
٤. وإن سقطت عن بُرْءٍ يُحِبُّ غَسْلُ ذلك الموضع خاصة بخلافِ ما إذا خَلَعَ أحد الحفين، حيث يلزمُهُ غَسْلُ الرِّجلين. الأحاديث وردت باشتراط كمال الطهارة وبالتوقيت في مسح الخفّ، ولم يرد نحو ذلك في مسح الجبيرة والعصابة. والثالث : إنّه إن سقطت الجبيرةُ عن موضعها لا يبطلُ . مسحه السابق، بخلاف الخف فإنّه إذا خرج القدم منه ولو بلا قصد بطل مسحه ؛ لسراية الحدث في القدم، فإنّ الخف عهد مانعاً ، فبزواله يسري الحدث إلى ما تحته فيجب غسله. وأما مسح الجبيرة فقائم مقام غسل ما تحته ، فصار كما إذا توضأ ثم حلق الشعر أو أزالَ الظفر أو قطع عضواً مغسولاً لا تجب إعادة الوضوء ولا يبطل وضوؤه.
والرابع : إنه إذا سقطت عن صحة فإنّما يجب غسل ذلك الموضع فقط دون الموضع الآخر، بخلاف مسح الخف ؛ فإنّ عند نزع أحدِ الحَفِّين يجب غسلُ الرِّجلِ الأخرى أيضاً كما من تحقيقه.
والخامس: إنه تصح الصلاة بدون مسح الجبيرة على رواية، ولا كذلك مسح الخفّ، وقد ذكر هذه المسألة.

Book of Purity: Wiping over Leather Socks. Parts. If he wiped, then removed them, then put them back on, he must repeat the wiping, but if he does not repeat, it suffices. And if it (the bandage) falls off and he replaces it with another, the better course is to repeat the wiping, but if he does not repeat, it suffices. And it is not required to repeat wiping of bandages three times; rather, once is sufficient, and this is the most correct. And one must know that wiping over a bandage differs from wiping over the khuff in: In al-‘Inayah: 'The difference between it and wiping the head and the khuff is that in them the majority is not required: because wiping the head is legislated by the Book and the letter ba entered upon the place, making it partial, and wiping over the khuff – if by the Book, its ruling is like the ruling of the conjunct, and if by the Sunnah, it required wiping part. As for wiping over bandages, it is established by the hadith of ‘Ali, and in it there is nothing indicating part, except that little is disregarded due to hardship.' [1] His saying: 'Ajjaza'u – meaning it suffices him; because what invalidates wiping is only its falling off or removal upon recovery, not without that; due to the existence of the excuse necessitating inability, so mere removal does not require repeating the wiping nor washing what is underneath. [2] His saying: 'Fabadalaha bi-ukhra' – meaning he tied a bandage or another splint in its place. [3] His saying: 'Wa huwa al-asah' – indication of negating the statement of some shaykhs that repetition is required because it is like washing, except if the wound is on the head. [4] His saying: 'Wa yajibu an ya‘lam...' etc. is an explanation of the existence of difference between wiping the khuff and wiping the bandage and the like, and they are many; the commentator mentioned four of them: First: It is not required for the validity of wiping the bandage that it be tied in a state of complete purity, while that is required for wiping the khuffs. Second: It is not limited to a time but is permitted until it falls off upon recovery, removing hardship, unlike wiping the khuff which is limited to a day and night or three days with their nights. The reason for that is... (1) End from al-‘Inayah (1:158-159). (2) Verified by Sahib al-Muhit (p. 374) and al-Durr al-Mukhtar (1:187), and see al-Bahr (1:198). 

Umdat al-Ri‘ayah ‘ala Sharh al-Wiqayah
1. It is permitted while in a state of impurity. 2. It is not assigned a duration. 3. If it falls off not upon recovery, wiping is not invalidated. 4. If it falls off upon recovery, washing that spot specifically is required, contrary to if one removes one of the khuffs, where washing both feet is obligatory. The hadiths came requiring complete purity and timing in wiping the khuff, and nothing similar came regarding wiping the bandage and cloth. Third: If the bandage falls from its place, his previous wiping is not invalidated, unlike the khuff: if the foot comes out of it even unintentionally, his wiping is invalidated because of the transmission of impurity to the foot; for the khuff was a barrier, so with its removal the impurity transmits to what is under it, so washing is obligatory. As for wiping the bandage, it stands in place of washing what is under it, so it becomes like if he performed wudu then shaved hair, or removed a nail, or cut a washed limb: wudu is not required to be repeated nor is his wudu invalidated. Fourth: If it falls off upon healing, only that spot must be washed, not the other foot, unlike wiping the khuff: when one khuff is removed, the other foot must also be washed as established. Fifth: On one narration, prayer is valid without wiping the bandage, not so for wiping the khuff, and this issue has been mentioned. Except for the one in minor impurity as passed. Sixth: It is permitted for both the one in minor impurity and the junub, unlike wiping the khuff, which is not permitted except for the one in minor impurity. Seventh: On one narration, covering all is required, unlike wiping the khuff. Eighth: By consensus, intention is not required for it, unlike wiping the khuff, where intention is required on one narration. Likewise the truth. Ninth: It is permitted to combine wiping the bandage of one foot and washing the other foot, and that is not permitted in wiping the khuff. Tenth: It is permitted to wipe over the bandage even if it is on other than the feet, unlike wiping the khuff. And there are other aspects as well, their total reaching more than thirty points, which al-Si‘ayah has detailed; whoever wishes to see them along with their pros and cons should refer to it.
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage from Umdat al-Ri‘ayah explains the differences between wiping over the khuff (leather socks) and wiping over the jabirah (bandage/splint) in Hanafi fiqh. The author begins by stating a ruling: if one wipes over the khuff, then removes it, then puts it back on, one must repeat the wipe; but if one does not, it still suffices. Similarly, if a bandage falls and is replaced, repeating the wipe is better but not required. Then he notes that wiping the jabirah three times is not required; once is sufficient. The core of the passage is a list of differences, drawn from al-‘Inayah and expanded by the commentator. The first difference is that for the jabirah, it is not required to be tied in a state of complete purity (i.e., while having wudu), whereas for the khuff, that is a condition. Second, wiping the jabirah has no time limit; it lasts until the wound heals, while khuff wiping is limited to one day and night for a resident and three for a traveler, based on hadiths. Third, if the bandage falls off before recovery, the previous wipe is not invalidated; if the khuff comes off even unintentionally, the wipe is invalidated because the foot is now exposed. Fourth, if the bandage falls after recovery, only that spot needs washing, whereas removing one khuff requires washing both feet. Fifth, on one narration, prayer is valid without wiping the jabirah, but not without wiping the khuff (if one is in a state of minor impurity). Sixth, wiping the jabirah is permitted for both a person in minor impurity and a junub, but wiping the khuff is only for minor impurity. Seventh, on one narration, covering the entire bandage is required for jabirah, but not for khuff. Eighth, intention (niyyah) is not required for jabirah by consensus, but it is required for khuff on one narration. Ninth, one can combine wiping a bandage on one foot and washing the other foot, which is not allowed for khuff. Tenth, the jabirah can be on any body part, not just feet, unlike khuff. The author notes there are over thirty differences, referring to al-Si‘ayah for details. This thorough comparison helps students understand the distinct legal frameworks for these two forms of wiping, rooted in different proofs: khuff wiping is based on the Sunnah with strict conditions, while jabirah wiping is based on the hadith of ‘Ali with more leniency due to hardship (haraj).

Key Terms
الخف (al-khuff): Leather socks worn over the feet, over which wiping is permitted instead of washing them during wudu under specific conditions.
الجبيرة (al-jabirah): A bandage or splint applied to a wound or broken bone, over which wiping is permitted instead of washing the injured area due to hardship.
المسح (al-mash): The act of wiping a wet hand over a limb or covering as a substitute for washing in wudu or ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is a correct difference between wiping over khuff and wiping over jabirah?
A) Wiping over jabirah requires intention by consensus, while khuff does not.
B) Wiping over jabirah is limited to one day and night.
C) Wiping over jabirah is permitted for both minor impurity and janabah, while khuff is only for minor impurity.
D) If the bandage falls off before healing, the wudu is invalidated just like when a khuff comes off.

Key Takeaway
Wiping over jabirah (bandage) is more lenient than wiping over khuff (leather socks) due to the absence of strict conditions in its proof and the overriding principle of removing hardship (haraj).

Ten Differences Between Wiping Over a Bandage and Leather Socks
Lists ten differences between masah al-jabirah and masah al-khuff

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and explain ten differences between masah al-jabirah and masah al-khuff, and apply the correct ruling in each scenario.

Core Idea
Wiping over a bandage is more flexible than over khuffs: no time limit, no intention required.

In Hanafi fiqh, both jabirah (bandage) and khuff (leather sock) are objects over which masah (wiping) is permitted as a substitute for washing. However, their rulings are not identical. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah outlines ten key differences that highlight the unique legal nature of each. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly performing wudu and prayer when such concessions are needed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objects
Recognize that the passage compares rulings for wiping over a bandage (jabirah) and wiping over leather socks (khuff). Both are concessions in wudu, but with different conditions.
Jabirah covers wounds; khuff covers the foot.
2
Work through each difference sequentially
For each of the ten differences listed, note the specific ruling for jabirah and the contrasting ruling for khuff. Pay attention to why each ruling applies.
The differences cover permissibility, time limits, effects of removal, coverage, intention, and more.
3
Understand the underlying rationale (talil)
The passage provides some reasoning, e.g., the khuff acts as a barrier, while jabirah substitutes for washing the wound. This affects rulings like invalidation upon removal.
The hadiths for khuff include time limit and complete purification; no similar reports for jabirah.
4
Apply the knowledge to practical scenarios
Given a situation involving a bandage or khuff, determine which set of rulings applies. For example, a junub can wipe jabirah but not khuff.
This step ensures the student can operationalize the differences.

Commentary
This passage is a list of ten differences between wiping over a bandage (jabirah) and wiping over leather socks (khuff) in Hanafi fiqh. The first difference: wiping over a bandage is allowed for any impurity event (minor hadath), whereas wiping over khuff has specific conditions. Second: no time limit for bandage wiping, while khuff has a time limit. Third: if the bandage falls off without healing, the wiping remains valid; but if the foot comes out of the khuff, wiping is invalidated. Fourth: if it falls off after healing, only that spot needs washing, but if one khuff is removed, both feet must be washed. Fifth: prayer without wiping the bandage is valid in one narration, not so for khuff. Sixth: bandage wiping is allowed for both minor impurity and janabah, but khuff wiping is only for minor impurity. Seventh: wiping must cover the entire bandage in one narration, not so for khuff. Eighth: intention is not required for bandage wiping by consensus, but required for khuff in one narration. Ninth: one can wipe the bandage on one foot and wash the other, but khuff requires both feet to be wiped. Tenth: bandage can be on any body part, khuff only on feet. The passage ends by noting there are more than thirty differences, referring to al-Si'aya for details.

Key Terms
جَبِيرَة (jabirah): Bandage or splint used to cover a wound or fracture; its wiping in wudu is a substitute for washing the affected part.
خُفّ (khuff): Leather sock worn on the foot over the wudu washing; wiping over it is permissible instead of washing the feet in specific circumstances.
مَسْح (masah): Wiping; in this context, running wet hands over a bandage or khuff as a substitute for washing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Wiping over a bandage differs from wiping over khuffs in at least ten key respects, including time limit, coverage, intention, and permissibility for junub.

The Hanafi Definition of Hayd and Its Three Types
Defines hayd and distinguishes it from istihadah and nifas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of hayd and identify its three essential components: blood, from the womb, from a mature woman.

Core Idea
Hayd is female-specific blood from the womb of a woman at least nine years old.

This lesson begins the chapter on menstruation and postpartum bleeding. You will learn the precise legal definition of hayd as given by the Hanafi school, and understand how it differs from other types of female bleeding. The commentary explains why this definition matters for many fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of female-specific blood
Read the first sentence: 'The bloods specific to women are three: menstruation, abnormal uterine bleeding, and postpartum bleeding.' This is the broad categorization.
Only these three types are considered in this chapter.
2
Locate the definition of hayd and list its components
Find the sentence: 'Menstruation is blood that the womb of a mature woman discharges.' Break it down into its core elements: (1) blood, (2) that discharges (exits), (3) from the womb, (4) of a mature woman.
'Mature woman' is specified as a girl of nine years.
3
Understand the restrictions in the definition
The commentary explains each restriction: 'specific to women' excludes nosebleeds etc.; 'blood' excludes other fluids; 'discharges' excludes internal blood; 'womb' excludes other organs; 'woman' excludes menstruating animals.
These restrictions are essential to avoid confusion with other bleedings.
4
Recognize why hayd is given more importance in the title
The text notes that hayd occurs more frequently and has more rulings, so it is the main subject of the chapter, with istihadah and nifas as subsidiary.
This explains the chapter title focusing on hayd.

باب الحيض والنفاس
الدماء المختصة بالنِّساء ) ثلاثة : حيض، واستحاضة، ونفاس. فالحيض: (هو دم ينفُضُهُ رَحمُ امرأة بالغة) : أي بنت تسع سنين [١]قوله : باب الحيض (۱) ؛ أي هذا باب في بيان أحكام الحيض ونحوه، وإنما اقتصر على ذكره في العنوان مع أنه ذكر حكم الاستحاضة والنفاس أيضاً في هذا الباب ؛ لكونه أكثر وقوعاً بالنسبة إليهما، وكون مسائله كثيرة وفروعه غفيرة بالنسبة إلى مسائلهما ، فكأنه هو الأهم المقصود، وما عداه تبع له. والسبب في تأخيره عما سبق أن ما سبق كان بياناً لأحكام الطهارة من الأحداث أصلاً وخلفاً، وكانت أكثر وقوعاً وأشمل للرِّجالِ والنِّساء، فصارت أحق بالتقديم من أحكام الطهارة من الأنجاس ، ومن الأحكام الخاصة بالنساء، وفي المقام نكات ولطائف

امرأة احترز به عن دمٍ يخرج من رحم الحيوانات التي تحيض كالخفاش والأرنب
والضبع ؛ فإنه لا يسمى حيضا شرعا.
۱۱) قال العلامة الشرنبلالي في مراقي الفلاح» (۱۳۸) : «الحيض من غوامض الأبواب وأعظم المهمات لأحكام كثيرة كالطلاق والعتاق والاستبراء والعدة والنسب وحل الوطء والصلاة
والصوم وقراءة القرآن ومسه والاعتكاف ودخول المسجد وطواف الحج والبلوغ».
Chapter on Menstruation and Postpartum Bleeding. The bloods specific to women are three: menstruation, abnormal uterine bleeding (istihadah), and postpartum bleeding (nifas). Menstruation is blood that the womb of a mature woman discharges; i.e., a girl of nine years [1] His statement: 'Chapter on Menstruation' means this is a chapter explaining the rulings of menstruation and similar matters. He only mentioned it in the title even though he also mentions the ruling of istihadah and nifas in this chapter, because menstruation occurs more frequently than the other two, and its issues are many and its branches abundant compared to their issues, so it is the main point intended, and the others are subsidiary to it. The reason for its delay after what preceded is that what preceded was an explanation of the rulings of purification from impurities (ahdath) originally and alternatively, and they occur more often and are more general for men and women, so they are more deserving of precedence over the rulings of purification from najasah (impurities) and the rulings specific to women. There are subtle points and elegant details here, the place for their elaboration is al-Si'aya. [2] His statement: 'specific to women' is a restriction to exclude blood from nosebleeds, phlebotomy, and the like that are common to men and women. [3] His statement: 'it is blood' – this is its legally valid definition, and linguistically it is an expression of absolute flowing; 'tandafiduhu' (discharges) with damma on the fa' from the root nafd, which is moving something to make what is on it (like dust) fall off: i.e., it expels it. This excludes the blood that is in the womb and has not exited to the external vagina, for that is not menstruation. 'Womb' – with fat-ha on the ra' and kasr on the ha', or vice versa – is the organ in which the child grows and the sperm settles. This excludes blood from other body parts. 'Woman' excludes blood that exits from the womb of animals that menstruate like bats, rabbits, and hyenas, for that is not called menstruation legally. [11] The scholar al-Sharnablali said in Maraqi al-Falah (138): 'Menstruation is one of the obscure chapters and the greatest of important matters due to many rulings such as divorce, emancipation, istibra', iddah, lineage, permissibility of intercourse, prayer, fasting, reciting the Quran, touching it, i'tikaf, entering the mosque, tawaf of hajj, and reaching puberty.'
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the chapter on menstruation (hayd) and postpartum bleeding (nifas) in a Hanafi fiqh text. It first states that three types of blood are specific to women: hayd, istihadah (irregular uterine bleeding), and nifas (postpartum blood). Then it defines hayd as blood that the womb of a mature woman discharges, specifying that maturity here means a girl who has reached nine years. The commentary explains why the chapter is titled only with hayd even though it includes istihadah and nifas: because hayd is more frequent and has more rulings. The delay of this chapter after previous ones is because the earlier chapters dealt with purification from hadath (states of impurity) which are more common and apply to both men and women. The commentary then dissects the definition: 'specific to women' excludes blood common to men and women (like nosebleeds); 'blood' excludes other fluids; 'discharges' means it exits, so internal blood is not hayd; 'womb' excludes blood from other organs; 'woman' excludes animals that menstruate. Finally, a quote from al-Sharnablali emphasizes the importance of hayd due to its many implications in various areas of fiqh.

Key Terms
حَيْض (hayd): Menstrual blood discharged from the womb of a woman who has reached nine years of age.
اسْتِحَاضَة (istihadah): Abnormal uterine bleeding that is not menstrual; it does not prevent prayer, fasting, or marital relations.
نِفاس (nifas): Postpartum bleeding after childbirth, with rulings similar to hayd for the duration.
رَحِم (rahim): Womb; the organ where the fetus develops, mentioned to specify that only blood from the womb is considered hayd.
بَالِغَة (balighah): Mature woman; in this context specifically a girl who has reached nine years of age.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Hayd is legally defined as blood discharged from the womb of a woman who has reached nine years, and it is distinct from istihadah and nifas.

Definition of Hayd and Its Conditions
Defining hayd and its conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of hayd and identify its three conditions (age, no disease, not menopause).

Core Idea
Hayd is natural uterine blood from a woman aged 9+ without disease, before menopause.

This lesson unpacks the foundational Hanafi definition of menstruation (hayd), distinguishing it from istihadah and nifas. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya provides the matn and its commentary, clarifying each condition. Understanding this definition is crucial because hayd triggers many rulings in purification, prayer, fasting, and family law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of blood specific to women
The text states that bloods specific to women are three: hayd (menstruation), istihadah (irregular bleeding), and nifas (postpartum bleeding). Recognizing these categories is the first step.
2
Parse the definition of hayd into its components
Hayd is defined as 'blood that the uterus of an adult woman expels.' Break down each term: 'blood' excludes internal bleeding; 'uterus' excludes other organs; 'woman' excludes animals; 'adult' implies age of puberty.
3
Apply the three conditions: age, no disease, not menopause
The text adds conditions: the woman must be at least 9 years old (age of puberty), the blood must be discharged naturally (not due to disease), and she must not have reached menopause (approx. 55). Blood failing any condition is istihadah.
The commentator clarifies that the key is whether the discharge is natural or due to disease, not the health of the uterus.
4
Distinguish hayd from istihadah using a hypothetical
Apply the definition: if a woman bleeds due to a medical condition, it is istihadah; if natural, it is hayd. Continuous bleeding may be part hayd and part istihadah depending on the cause at different times.

Chapter of Menstruation and Postpartum Bleeding. It is blood that the uterus of an adult woman expels. Chapter of Menstruation and Postpartum Bleeding. [1] The bloods specific to women are three: menstruation, irregular bleeding (istihadah), and postpartum bleeding. So menstruation: is blood that the uterus of an adult woman expels, meaning a girl of nine years. [1] His statement: 'Chapter of Menstruation' — meaning this is a chapter on explaining the rulings of menstruation and similar matters. He only mentioned it in the title despite also mentioning the ruling of istihadah and postpartum bleeding in this chapter, because it occurs more frequently relative to them, and its issues are many and its branches numerous compared to their issues, so it is as if it is the main intended topic, and what is other than it is subordinate. The reason for its delay after what preceded is that what preceded was an explanation of the rulings of purification from hadath (impurity) original and substitute, and they occur more frequently and are general to men and women, so they deserved precedence over the rulings of purification from impurities (najasat) and over the rulings specific to women. In this context there are subtleties and felicities whose place of exposition is 'al-Si'ayah'. [2] His statement: 'specific to women' — he excludes by this restriction blood from nosebleed, phlebotomy, and the like, which is common to men and women. [3] His statement: 'it is blood' — this is its legally valid interpretation. Linguistically, it is an expression of absolute flowing. 'Yanfuduhu' (expels) with a damma on the fa from al-nafd, which is moving something to cause what is on it of dust and the like to fall, meaning it discharges it. He excludes by it the blood that is in the uterus and has not come out to the external vulva, for it is not menstruation. 'Rahim' (uterus) — with fatha on the ra and kasra on the ha, and the reverse — an organ in which the child develops and the sperm settles. He excludes by it the blood of other organs. 'Imra'ah' (woman) — he excludes by it blood that comes out from the uterus of animals that menstruate, such as the bat, rabbit, and hyena; for it is not called menstruation legally. 11) Al-'Allamah al-Shurunbulali said in 'Maraqi al-Falah': 'Menstruation is among the subtlest chapters and the greatest important matters due to many rulings such as divorce, manumission, istibra', waiting period, lineage, permissibility of intercourse, prayer, fasting, reciting Quran, touching it, i'tikaf, entering the mosque, tawaf of Hajj, and reaching puberty.' There is no disease in her, and she has not reached the age of menopause. There is no disease in her, and she has not reached the age of menopause. So what is not from the uterus is not menstruation, and likewise what is before the age of puberty, i.e., nine years, and likewise what the uterus expels due to disease. So if blood continues, some flow is natural, so it is menstruation, and some flow due to disease, so it is not menstruation. 'Balighah' (adult): i.e., one who has reached a certain age and if she claimed puberty, she is believed. This refutes the objection that knowledge of puberty is dependent on menstruation, so defining it with it is circular. This is a precaution against blood of one who is not like that, as the commentator will mention. 'La da'a biha' (there is no disease in her): a description of woman — i.e., no illness in her — a precaution against blood of the uterus coming out due to disease, for it is istihadah. 'Wa lam tablugh' (and she has not reached): i.e., that woman has not reached the age of menopause. 'Iyas' (menopause) originally is 'aiyas' on the pattern 'if'al' from 'ai'asa' meaning I made him cut off hope; the hamza which is the second radical was omitted for lightness. As in al-Mughrib. This is a precaution against blood of the age of menopause; for it is istihadah. [1] His statement: 'there is no disease in her' — it would be more apparent to say 'not due to disease' to convey that blood coming from the uterus due to disease is not menstruation. His expression implies that the uterus that has disease — the blood coming from it is not menstruation, but it is not so. Indeed, the diseased uterus: if the discharge of blood from it is natural, it is menstruation; otherwise no. In summary, what matters is the discharge due to disease or not, not the uterus being diseased or healthy. [2] His statement: 'due to disease' — he points out that what matters in it being menstruation or not is its discharge according to natural disposition, and its discharge due to disease, whether the uterus itself is diseased or healthy, as we pointed out previously. His statement: 'so if it continues' — the purpose is to explain that menstruation and istihadah may coincide in one blood at different times. (1) 'al-Mughrib' (p. 510).
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage defines hayd (menstruation) and its conditions from the Hanafi perspective. It opens with the base text (matn) stating that blood specific to women is of three types: hayd, istihadah, and nifas. Hayd is defined as blood expelled by the uterus of an adult woman, meaning a girl who has reached at least nine years of age. The commentary (sharh) elaborates on each term of the definition. The phrase 'specific to women' excludes bleeding common to both sexes, like nosebleeds. 'Blood' excludes blood that remains inside the uterus and has not exited to the external vulva. 'Uterus' distinguishes it from blood of other organs, and 'woman' excludes blood from animals that menstruate, like bats. The condition 'there is no disease in her' means the bleeding is due to natural constitution, not illness, and 'she has not reached menopause' sets an upper age limit. The commentator clarifies that the key factor is whether the discharge is natural or due to disease, not whether the uterus itself is healthy or diseased. If blood continues, part may be hayd and part istihadah depending on the cause at different times. This definition serves as the foundation for many rulings in purification, prayer, fasting, marriage, and other areas.

Key Terms
حيض (hayd): Blood that the uterus of an adult woman (9 years or older, not due to disease, not post-menopause) expels naturally.
استحاضة (istihadah): Blood from the uterus due to disease or other causes, not natural menstruation; it occurs outside regular menstrual periods or beyond normal duration.
نفاس (nifas): Postpartum bleeding after childbirth, a separate category from hayd.
بالغة (balighah): A woman who has reached the age of puberty, which is at least 9 years by the Hanafi view.
إياس (iyas): Age of menopause, when a woman stops menstruating naturally; defined as approximately 55 years in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition in this text, which condition is necessary for blood to be considered hayd?
A) The woman must be married.
B) The blood must come from the uterus.
C) The woman must be at least 15 years old.
D) The blood must be continuous for at least three days.

Key Takeaway
Hayd is defined by three conditions: minimum age 9, natural discharge, and pre-menopause; any uterine bleeding failing these is istihadah.

Taharah — Purification
advanced

Refining the Definition of Hayd: Conditions, Exclusions, and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Critically refining the definition of hayd by analyzing its conditions and exclusions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the refined Hanafi definition of hayd, identify the three key exclusions (non-uterine, before puberty, due to illness), explain why the matn's wording 'لا داء بها' is imprecise, and distinguish between the two positions regarding blood after menopause.

Core Idea
Hayd is defined by natural exit, not womb health; nifas must be excluded; menopause blood status is disputed within the school.

The Hanafi definition of hayd (menstruation) is more than a simple description—it is a legal tool that distinguishes natural menstrual bleeding from pathological (istihadah) and postnatal (nifas) bleeding. The matn of Al-Wiqayah offers a concise definition, but the commentator in 'Umda al-Ri'ayah subjects it to rigorous scrutiny, refining both its wording and its conditions. This lesson walks through that refinement, from the critique of the phrase 'لا داء بها' to the necessary exclusion of nifas and the unresolved dispute over blood after menopause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn's definition and its immediate exclusions
Begin by reading the core definition: 'She has no disease and has not reached menopause.' Then note the three explicit exclusions: blood not from the womb, blood before puberty (9 years), and blood due to illness. Understand that these exclusions define what is not hayd.
The commentator explains that if bleeding continues, part may be natural hayd and part istihadah.
2
Critique the wording 'لا داء بها'
The commentator argues that the phrase 'she has no disease' is imprecise. It suggests that a diseased womb cannot produce hayd, but the correct criterion is the cause of the blood: natural vs. due to illness. An ill womb can still produce natural hayd if the blood exits according to nature.
The more precise wording would be 'لا لمرض' (not due to illness).
3
Analyze the need for an additional condition: absence of childbirth
The definition as given could include nifas (postpartum bleeding) because linguistically 'hayd' is sometimes used for nifas. The commentator insists that a separate condition of 'no childbirth' is needed to clearly exclude nifas, as it has its own distinct legal reality.
The objection that 'no disease' suffices is rejected; childbirth is not an illness legally or customarily.
4
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on menopause blood
The commentator presents two conflicting fatwa terms: 'al-ah' (most correct) says blood after menopause is absolutely not hayd; 'al-mukhtar' (chosen) says strong blood after menopause is hayd. Both are considered fatwa-worthy, creating a practical contradiction.
The passage does not resolve the contradiction, merely highlights it.
5
Synthesize the refined definition
From the commentary, construct the complete refined definition: 'Hayd is blood that exits naturally from the womb of a woman who has reached puberty, has not reached menopause, is not due to illness, and is not from childbirth.'
This definition excludes istihadah, nifas, and blood before puberty or after menopause (though the latter is disputed).

Source Text

كتاب الطهارة الحيض
وكما قيده بعدمِ الدَّاء، يَجِبُ أن يقيده بعدم الولادة أيضاً احترازاً عن النفاس ثم الأصح) أن الحيض مؤقت إلى سن الإياس
قوله: احترازاً؛ أي للاحتراز عن النفاس، هو بالكسر لغة: عبارة عن الولادة، وشرعاً عبارة عن دم خارج من رحم عقيب خروج ولد، وحاصل إيراده: أن تعريف المصنف له غير مانع بصدقه على النفاس، فيجب أن يذكر قيد عدم الولادة؛ لإخراجه. فإن قلت: قد يطلقُ الحيض على النفاس أيضاً وقد ورد ذلك في أحاديث كثيرة، وبوب له البخاري له في «صحيحه» باباً على حدة، فلا بأس في صدق تعريف الحيض على النفاس مع أن المصنّف لا يحتمل أن يكون أراد بالحيض ما يعم النفاس، فلا حاجة إلى قيد زائد، بل زيادته تصرح به.
قلت: إطلاق الحيض على النفاس وبالعكس ليس بمستنكر، لكن الكلام في أنّ لكل منهما حقيقة متمايزة عن الآخر، وسيذكر المصنّف له حكم النفاس على حدة، وهو شاهد عدل على أنه أراد بالحيض هو ما يقابلُ النفاس والاستحاضة، فحينئذ يجب تقييده بما يخرج النفاس.
فإن قلت: قوله: «لا داءَ بها كافٍ لخروج النفاس، فإن الولادة في حكم المرض.
قلت: كلا؛ فإنّ كونَ الولادة مرضاً مستنكر جداً شرعاً وعرفاً.
قوله: ثم الأصح (1)؛ يعني الأصح أنَّ الحيض المعتبر شرعاً مؤقت إلى سنّ الإياس، فإذا بلغت المرأةُ ذلك السنّ ورأت الدم بعده لا يعد ذلك حيضاً. فإن قلت: هذا الذي ذكره من الأصح يدلّ على أن ما خرج بعد سن الإياس ليس بحيض مطلقاً، وهو منافٍ لما وسمه بلفظ: «المختار» من أن الـدم القوي بعده حيض، ومن المعلوم أن المختار والأصح كلاهما من ألفاظ الفتوى، فكيف يصح جعلُ كل مفتی به، مع تخالفهما؟
(1) وصححه ـه أيضاً في «البحر الرائق»(1:201).
She has no disease, and she has not reached menopause. So what is not from the womb is not menstruation, and likewise what is before the age of puberty, i.e., nine years, and likewise what the womb expels due to illness. So if bleeding continues, some flow is natural, so it is menstruation, and some flow is due to illness, so it is not menstruation. 'Balighah' (puberty): i.e., she who has reached an age and affirmed that she has reached puberty, she is believed. Thus the objection that knowledge of puberty depends on menstruation, making the definition circular, is repelled. This is precaution against the blood of one who is not such, as the commentator will mention. 'No disease in her': a description of a woman—i.e., no illness in her—a precaution against blood from the womb exiting due to illness, for it is istihadah. 'And she has not reached': i.e., that woman has reached the age of menopause (iyas). It is originally 'al-iyanas' on the pattern of if'al, from ayasah: I made him cut off hope; the hamzah which is the medial root letter is omitted for lightness. Thus in Al-Mughrib. And this is a precaution against blood of the age of menopause, for it is istihadah. His statement: 'No disease in her': the more apparent is that he should say 'not due to illness', to indicate that what exits from the womb due to illness is not menstruation. His wording suggests that the womb which has illness, the blood exiting from it is not menstruation, but this is not so. For the ill womb, if the blood exits naturally, it is menstruation; otherwise not. In summary, what matters is the exit due to illness or not, not whether the womb is ill or healthy. His statement: 'due to illness': he indicated by it that what matters in it being menstruation or not is its exit according to nature, and its exit due to illness, whether the womb itself is ill or healthy, as we indicated earlier. His statement: 'so if it continues': the purpose of it is to explain that menstruation and istihadah may combine in one blood at different times. (1) Al-Mughrib (p.510). Book of Purity: Menstruation. And just as he qualified it with absence of disease, he must also qualify it with absence of childbirth, as a precaution against nifas. Then the most correct is that menstruation is limited to the age of menopause. His statement: 'precaution': i.e., for precaution against nifas. Nifas (with kasr) linguistically means childbirth, and legally means blood exiting from the womb after the delivery of a child. The gist of his objection is that the author's definition is not preventive because it applies to nifas, so he must mention the condition of no childbirth to exclude it. If you say: The term menstruation is also applied to nifas, and this has occurred in many hadiths, and al-Bukhari devoted a separate chapter in his Sahih to it, so there is no harm in the definition applying to nifas, especially since the author cannot be intended to mean menstruation that includes nifas, so no extra condition is needed; rather adding it would make it explicit. I say: Applying menstruation to nifas and vice versa is not objectionable, but the point is that each has a distinct legal reality. The author will mention the ruling of nifas separately, and that is a valid witness that he meant by menstruation what is opposed to nifas and istihadah. Therefore, it is necessary to qualify it to exclude nifas. If you say: His statement 'she has no disease' is sufficient to exclude nifas, because childbirth is in the category of illness. I say: No, for considering childbirth an illness is highly objectionable legally and customarily. His statement: 'then the most correct (al-ah)': meaning the most correct is that legally significant menstruation is limited to the age of menopause. So when a woman reaches that age and sees blood after it, that is not counted as menstruation. If you say: This statement of 'al-ah' indicates that what exits after the age of menopause is absolutely not menstruation, which contradicts what he labeled as 'al-mukhtar' (the chosen) that strong blood after it is menstruation. And it is known that both al-ah and al-mukhtar are terms used for fatwa, so how can both be considered mufta bihi, despite their opposition? (1) He also corrected it in Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (1:201).
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the definition of menstruation (hayd) as presented in the base text (matn) of Al-Wiqayah. The commentator, the author of 'Umda al-Ri'aya, opens by repeating the matn: 'She has no disease, and she has not reached menopause.' He then explains that blood not originating from the womb is not hayd, nor is blood before the age of puberty (nine years), nor blood expelled due to illness. If bleeding continues, part may be natural (hayd) and part pathological (istihadah). The term 'balighah' (puberty) is defined, and a circularity objection is refuted: the definition does not rely on hayd to define puberty. The condition 'no disease' is a precaution against blood caused by illness (istihadah). The condition 'not reached menopause' excludes blood after the age of menopause (sinn al-iyas), which is also istihadah. The commentator then criticizes the matn's wording 'no disease in her', arguing that it should be 'not due to illness' to correctly indicate that the criterion is the cause of bleeding, not the health of the womb. He explains that an ill womb can still produce natural hayd if the blood flows naturally. Next, he discusses the necessity of adding a condition 'absence of childbirth' to exclude nifas (postpartum bleeding). He presents a dialectical exchange: an objector says the term hayd can apply to nifas linguistically and in hadith, so no extra condition is needed. The commentator replies that each has a distinct legal reality, and since the author later gives separate rulings for nifas, it is clear that hayd is meant to exclude it. Another objection claims that 'no disease' already excludes nifas because childbirth is like an illness. The commentator firmly rejects this, calling the equation of childbirth with illness 'highly objectionable' both legally and customarily. Finally, the passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute: whether blood after menopause can be hayd. The commentator states that 'the most correct' (al-ah) view is that hayd is limited to before menopause. However, he notes that the same author elsewhere uses 'al-mukhtar' (the chosen) to say that strong blood after menopause is hayd. This is a contradiction between two fatwa terms. The commentator acknowledges the problem but does not resolve it here, merely pointing out the inconsistency.

Key Terms
حيض (hayd): Blood from the womb that exits naturally (not due to illness) and within the menstruating age (between puberty and menopause).
استحاضة (istihāḍah): Blood from the womb due to illness or other causes, not meeting the conditions of hayd or nifas.
نفاس (nifās): Blood exiting from the womb after childbirth, considered a separate category from hayd.
إياس (iyās): The age of menopause, after which a woman no longer menstruates; in the Hanafi school, typically set at 55 years.
داء (dā’): Disease or illness; the absence of disease is a condition for hayd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why is the matn's phrase 'لا داء بها' ('no disease in her') not the most precise wording?
A) Because it implies that an ill womb cannot produce menstrual blood, whereas an ill womb can produce natural hayd if the blood flows naturally.
B) Because the phrase should be 'لا داء بها ولا ولادة' to also exclude nifas.
C) Because the word 'داء' is ambiguous and can mean different things.
D) Because the phrase should be 'لا مرض بها' (no illness in her) to be consistent with later usage.

Key Takeaway
The refined Hanafi definition of hayd requires blood to be natural (not due to illness), from the womb, after puberty, before menopause, and not from childbirth; the intra-Hanafi dispute over menopause blood remains unresolved in this text.

Defining Menstruation: Excluding Postpartum Bleeding
Commentary defending the need to exclude postpartum blood from the definition of menstruation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of hayd, identify the two exclusions (disease and childbirth), and explain why nifas must be explicitly excluded.

Core Idea
Postpartum bleeding (nifas) is a distinct legal category from menstruation (hayd) and must be explicitly excluded from hayd's definition.

This passage is a critical refinement of a base text's definition of menstruation. The commentator argues that the simple definition 'blood without disease' is not exclusive enough; it would incorrectly include postpartum bleeding (nifas). By examining the objection and response, we learn the precise contours of the Hanafi legal definition of hayd and why precision matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base definition
The base text defined hayd as blood that exits from a woman's womb without disease. Recognize that this is the starting point for the commentator's critique.
The base definition is: 'دم يخرج من رحم المرأة من غير داء'.
2
Understand the objection
The commentator points out that this definition is not exclusive (ghayr mani') because it also applies to nifas (postpartum bleeding). To exclude nifas, the condition 'without childbirth' must be added.
The objector (fictitious) questions why a separate condition is needed since the terms are sometimes used interchangeably in hadith.
3
Follow the refutation
The commentator refutes by stating that despite occasional linguistic overlap, each term has its own legal reality (haqiqah mutamayyizah). The fact that the author later treats nifas separately confirms that hayd is intended to exclude it.
The refutation also rejects the idea that childbirth is a disease, stressing that such a notion is objectionable both legally and customarily.
4
Extract the refined definition
The refined definition of hayd becomes: 'Blood that exits from the womb of a woman of menstruating age, not due to disease and not due to childbirth.' This explicitly excludes istihadah (disease) and nifas (childbirth).
This precision is essential for correct application of rulings related to prayer, fasting, and ritual purity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة الحيض

وكما قيده بعدمِ الدَّاء ، يَجِبُ أن يقيده بعدم الولادة أيضاً احترازاً عن النفاس

[1] قوله : احترازاً ؛ أي للاحتراز عن النفاس، هو بالكسر لغة : عبارة عن الولادة، وشرعاً عبارة عن دم خارج من رحم عقيب خروج ولد، وحاصل إيراده : أن تعريف المصنف له غير مانع بصدقه على النفاس ، فيجب أن يذكر قيد عدم الولادة ؛ لإخراجه. فإن قلت : قد يطلقُ الحيض على النفاس أيضاً وقد ورد ذلك في أحاديث كثيرة ، وبوب له البخاري له في «صحيحه» باباً على حدة، فلا بأس في صدق تعريف الحيض على النفاس مع أن المصنّف لا يحتمل أن يكون أراد بالحيض ما يعم النفاس ، فلا حاجة إلى قيد زائد ، بل زيادته تصرح به.
قلت : إطلاق الحيض على النفاس وبالعكس ليس بمستنكر، لكن الكلام في أنّ لكل منهما حقيقة متمايزة عن الآخر، وسيذكر المصنّف له حكم النفاس على حدة ، وهو شاهد عدل على أنه أراد بالحيض هو ما يقابلُ النفاس والاستحاضة، فحينئذ يجب تقييده بما يخرج النفاس.

فإن قلت : قوله : «لا داءَ بها كافٍ لخروج النفاس، فإن الولادة في حكم المرض.
قلت : كلا ؛ فإنّ كونَ الولادة مرضاً مستنكر جداً شرعاً وعرفاً.
Book of Purification: Menstruation. And just as he restricted it with the absence of disease, he must also restrict it with the absence of childbirth, as a precaution against postpartum bleeding. [1] His saying: 'as a precaution' – meaning for precaution against postpartum bleeding. Lexically, 'nifas' (with kasra) means childbirth, and legally it is blood that exits from the womb following the exit of a child. The gist of his objection is that the author's definition is not exclusive because it applies to postpartum bleeding, so one must mention the condition of not having childbirth to exclude it. If you say: Menstruation is sometimes also applied to postpartum bleeding, and that occurs in many hadiths, and Bukhari devoted a separate chapter to it in his Sahih, so there is no harm in the definition of menstruation applying to postpartum bleeding, especially since the author cannot be understood to mean menstruation that includes postpartum bleeding, so there is no need for an extra condition; rather, adding it would clarify. I say: The application of menstruation to postpartum bleeding and vice versa is not objectionable, but the issue is that each has a distinct reality from the other. The author will mention the ruling of postpartum bleeding separately, which is a just witness that he meant by menstruation that which is opposite of postpartum bleeding and istihadah. Therefore, it is necessary to restrict it with what excludes postpartum bleeding. If you say: His saying 'there is no disease in her' is sufficient to exclude postpartum bleeding, for childbirth is like illness. I say: No, because considering childbirth an illness is extremely objectionable legally and customarily.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the definition of menstruation (hayd) from a commentator on a Hanafi fiqh text. The author of the base text had defined menstruation as blood that exits from a woman's womb without disease. The commentator argues that this definition is not exclusive (ghayr mani') because it would also include postpartum bleeding (nifas), which is blood that exits after childbirth. To exclude nifas, the condition 'without childbirth' must be added. The commentator then addresses an objection: since the terms hayd and nifas are sometimes used interchangeably in hadith, why insist on a separate condition? The response is that despite occasional linguistic overlap, each has its own legal reality. The author of the base text will later treat nifas separately, confirming that he meant hayd to exclude nifas and istihadah (non-menstrual bleeding). Finally, the commentator rejects the idea that childbirth can be considered a disease, which would make the original phrase 'no disease' sufficient to exclude nifas. This discussion demonstrates the Hanafi concern for precise legal definitions to avoid ambiguity.

Key Terms
الحيض (al-hayd): Legally: blood that flows from the womb of a woman of menstruating age, not due to childbirth or disease.
النفاس (al-nifas): Legally: blood that exits from the womb following the birth of a child.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator insist on adding the condition 'after completing childbirth' to the definition of menstruation?
A) Because the original definition included postpartum bleeding, which is incorrect.
B) Because if one does not exclude postpartum bleeding, the definition of menstruation would also apply to postpartum bleeding, which is a separate category.
C) Because postpartum bleeding is considered a disease and thus must be excluded.
D) Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) defined menstruation in that way.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, hayd and nifas are distinct categories; a precise definition of hayd must explicitly exclude nifas to avoid conflation.
